Economics

* Services sector Businesses in the service sector reined back their price rises last month, the CIPS/RBS purchasing managers' index of the sector showed. Its headline measure of activity was 57.2, unchanged from April, on a scale where any figure above 50 indicates rising levels of activity. However, the index for prices charged dropped to 52.5, down from 53.9 in April and 55.3 in March. Although this suggested that prices continued to rise, the figure was the lowest since February 2006. Page 59

TD
* Consumer confidence picked up to its highest level in 18 months in May despite the affect of higher interest rates, Nationwide said. Its consumer confidence index rose by nine points to 99, the highest level since November 2005.

* Eurozone services sector growth, excluding retail, rose at a marginally faster rate than expected in May with employment at an 11-month high, while price pressures eased, according to the purchasing managers index. Its headline gauge of activity rose to 57.3, from 57 in April.

* Eurozone retail sales growth slowed in April, despite a boost from the recovering German economy. Data from Eurostat showed that retail volumes rose by 0.2 per cent and were up by just 1.6 per cent over the year, compared with a 2.6 per cent rise in the year to March.

* US economy Ben Bernanke, Chairman of the US Federal Reserve, said that growth in the US economy was likely to improve to a "moderate" pace after a slow start to the year. But he gave warning that the housing market slowdown was likely to have a longer-lasting impact on growth than previously thought. Page 60

* US non-manufacturing sector The US Institute for Supply Management's index of business activity in the non-manufacturing sector registered a pace of 59.7 in May, up from 56 in April and ahead of expectations. A reading above 50 indicates expansion.
* China should step up market opening and currency reforms to help to counter a "protectionist tide" in
America, said Henry Paulson, the US Treasury Secretary.

Up 0.6%
* High street banks Pressure on banks to give their customers a better service increased after a
European Union group highlighted the obstacles involved in opening or switching accounts. Page 59

* New York Stock Exchange The parent of the New York Stock Exchange has been accused in a $4 billion (Pounds 2.01 billion) lawsuit of providing prices through its electronic trading system that are inferior to those available to floor traders. Page 63
* Royal Bank of Scotland said it would not allow a multibillion-dollar potential lawsuit from Bank of
America to stymie its Euro 71 billion (Pounds 48.1 billion) bid for ABN Amro, the Dutch bank. Page 59

* Private equity industry Gordon Brown, the Chancellor, indicated at the GMB trade union conference that the private equity industry could face increased taxes as part of a government review. Page 48

* Vector Hospitality, the hotel real estate investment trust, looked to have rescued its Pounds 2 billion London flotation after reducing its price range to attract investors. It was forced to cut its price to between 875p and 900p, from the 995p to Pounds 11.15 range announced earlier. Page 57
Consumer goods
Up 0.7%

* Cadbury Schweppes, the confectionery and soft drinks group, has sold another batch of businesses as it remained on track to generate Pounds 250 million from disposals by the end of the year.

* Diageo, the world's biggest drinks group, was in danger of losing its liquor licence in South Korea after an inquiry into possible tax irregularities.

Up 1.1%

* General Motors, the US carmaker, has awarded two contracts to companies that will help to speed up the development of the Chevrolet Volt, its plug-in hybrid car. The group said that plans to introduce four new hybrid models this year.
Health
Down 0.8%

* GlaxoSmithKline, Europe's biggest drugs maker, stepped up its defence of Avandia, the diabetes medicine, after the release of fresh data that the company claimed showed little significant difference between the drug's safety record and that of other common diabetes treatments. Page 57
Industrials
Up 1%

* Alukbond India Private, the maker of aluminium composite panels, has seen a 76 per cent stake in it bought from its owner Navin Keswani by Alcan, the aluminium group. Financial terms were not disclosed.

Up 0.6%

* Rugby World Cup organisers have failed in an attempt to secure exclusive control over corporate hospitality at the event this year. The Paris Commercial Court has awarded Marcus Evans, the British events group, the right to run World Cup hospitality packages without the approval of the organising committee. Page 61
* Marriott International, the US hotelier, has teamed up with Nickelodeon, the entertainment group, and
Miller Global Properties to develop 20 resorts by 2020.

Up 0.7%

Pounds 190 million from Trinity Mirror, owner of the trade paper of the horse racing industry. Page 55

* World Association of Newspapers said that newspapers hoping to survive in the technological age must venture into the online and mobile phone spheres.

* Joost, the internet-based TV service, said that it had appointed Cisco veteran Mike Volpi as chief executive officer. He replaces Fredrik de Wahl, who will remain at Joost as chief strategy officer.

* Raw materials The  European Commission said that European Union members are facing a challenge over regional access to raw materials. Many metallic minerals are either geologically not available within the EU or only in relatively small volumes.
Retailing
Up 0.7%

* Gap, the US fashion retailer, has cut up to 90 jobs at its European headquarters in London. Many were linked to children's clothing, where Gap is dismantling its European design team. Page 59
* Carphone Warehouse, the mobile phone retailer, has won compensation from BT, the telecoms
group, for the debacle over its TalkTalk broadband service. It also reported full-year earnings of Pounds
294 million on revenues up 31 per cent to Pounds 3.9 billion and said it was close to overcoming its
broadband service problems. Page 57
Support services
Up 0.6%

* Dawson Holdings, the newspaper and magazine distributor, reported a 30 per cent fall in first-half pre-tax profits to Pounds 4.5 million, amid a slowdown in magazine sales and weaker margins on contracts won last year.

* Mice, the marketing services group that is based in Coventry, called for its shares to be suspended amid fears that it will have insufficient cash to continue trading unless it can secure additional funding.

* Interserve, the services, maintenance and building group, said it won a series of health projects and an educational Private Finance Initiative scheme worth more than Pounds 270 million.

* Christian Salvesen, the logistics and transport group that is based in Northampton, saw its full-year underlying pre-tax profits fall by 19 per cent to Pounds 12.5 million as its UK transport business increased its losses.

* Fox Hayes, the Leeds law firm, is in a court battle with the Financial Services Authority, the City regulator that wants to  fine the group Pounds 150,000 for alleged offences involving overseas "boiler rooms". The alleged regulatory breaches helped five boiler room firms to target investors who lost almost $21 million (Pounds 10.6 million), according to court documents. Page 59

Up 0.1%

* Apple The European Commission has extended the deadline for Apple, the US computer maker, to respond to its antitrust concerns over the pricing of songs on its iTunes online music store. Page 63

* Intel, the computer chip maker, is planning a $199 (Pounds 99.70) notebook computer and has teamed up with Asustek Computer, the Taiwanese circuit board maker. Page 63

* Dell, the computer maker, said that it is to discriminate between suppliers on the basis of how green they are and will blacklist those that do not curb their impact on the environment. Page 63

* ISoft The Australian healthcare group IBA which is trying to buy iSoft, the troubled NHS software supplier, has been told by its lawyers that it has grounds to sue the Government and the American contractor which is trying to block the deal. Page 61
Telecoms
Up 0.6%

* Telefonica,  the Spanish telecoms group, plans to invest more than Euro 10 billion (Pounds 6.8 billion) in Latin America up to 2009, which will be about one third of the group's predicted global investment for the period. The use of broadband in Latin America has grown at an average annual rate of 85 per cent.

HD        FT.com site : Brussels to fine Telefonica  over broadband margins. BY        Tobias Buck in Brussels and Mark?Mulligan in Madrid
WC       457 words
PD        21 June 2007
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA        English
CY        (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Telefonica,  Spain's leading telecoms group, will next month be punished and  fined by the European    Commission for abusing its dominant position in the market for broadband services.
The Brussels  antitrust watchdog issued formal charges against the group last year, accusing
Telefonica  of operating a "margin squeeze" on competing groups.

TD
The Commission found that the wholesale cost of accessing  Telefonica's  broadband network was so close to prices charged consumers that rivals were unable to earn a sufficient margin, effectively shutting them out of the broadband market.

According to people familiar with the case, the charges have been confirmed in a draft decision set to be approved by the Commission at a meeting on July 4.
The ruling was last Friday presented to national  competition regulators, who gave broad backing.

The Commission has yet to determine  Telefonica's  fine, but under EU rules it can impose a penalty of up to 10 per cent of the group's annual global turnover.

The case has raised questions about the system used by Spain's Telecoms Market Commission (CMT), the domestic regulator, to calculate wholesale broadband charges.

Its investigation found that they left scope for  competition, and Telefonica  has repeatedly pointed to the regulator's approval in its defence against the Commission accusations.

At the same time, the CMT and  Telefonica  are at loggerheads over sanctions against the group related to the alleged  abuse of its dominant position in fixed-line services.

In two cases involving margin squeezes in the telecoms sector - against France Telecom's  Wanadoo and Deutsche Telekom in 2003 - Brussels opted for relatively low  fines of 10.4m ($14m) and 12.6m respectively.

But since Neelie Kroes took over as EU  competition commissioner in 2004, the regulator has drastically increased fines for antitrust abuses, making it unlikely  Telefonica  will escape as lightly as its German and French rivals.

Last year the Commission imposed  fines of 1.85bn, a record that is certain to be topped in 2007, which has so far seen the regulator clock up just over 2bn in  fines.

Telefonica  stands accused of operating the margin squeeze since 2001.

Linking the alleged  abuse to the wider problems affecting the broadband market in Spain, Brussels said last year that "retail broadband prices in Spain are high and well above EU average".

The Commission stressed that the roll-out of broadband infrastructure to compete with  Telefonica's network was happening more slowly than in other European markets.
The group, which has always denied wrongdoing, is likely to appeal against any decision in front of the
European Court of First Instance, the EU's second-highest court.

Telefonica,  Spain's leading telecoms group, will next month be punished and  fined by the European   Commission for abusing its dominant position in the market for broadband services.
The Brussels  antitrust watchdog issued formal charges against the group last year, accusing
Telefonica  of operating a "margin squeeze" on competing groups.

TD
The Commission found that the wholesale cost of accessing  Telefonica's  broadband network was so close to prices charged consumers that rivals were unable to earn a sufficient margin, effectively shutting them out of the broadband market.

According to people familiar with the case, the charges have been confirmed in a draft decision set to be approved by the Commission at a meeting on July 4.

HD          Euro CDS: High/Low  Grade Sectors See Divergence
WC         732 words
PD          22 June 2007
ET          12:42 PM
SN          Market News International
SC          MARNEW
LA          English
CY          (c) 2007. Market News International, Inc.

TD



LONDON, June 22 (MNI) - Euro CDS spreads had a more composed
tone today with high beta indices returning to the middle of the week's range. iTraxx crossover opened at the 207bps level, after spiking as high as 216bps yesterday.

The
Bear Stearns/sub prime debacle that prompted the increased
volatility in low grade sectors this week appears to be reaching some
kind resolution with  Bear Stearns investment bank reportedly planning to take on $3.2 bln of loans from creditors in an effort to stave off moves to auction the collateral.

The news did not prompt a rally in the sector, though, with the
ABX low grade indices related to sub prime remaining close to the week's lows, even after the loan buyout proposal was announced.

This was the case in the wider market, where CDS related to this type of securitisation was trading up to 20bps wider on the week. GMAC and FMCC in Euros were notable examples of this, leading other related single names wider.

In the new issue ABS market, which has a pipeline of over $44bln this month, traders reported slower demand for deals viewed as risky, but increased demand for higher quality issues, to the extent that the better quality deals are being priced at even tighter levels.

This trend could lead to a general divergence of high and low grade credit in the coming weeks as investors have been forced to take a less gung ho stance in the wake of the  Bear Stearns hedge fund problems.

Late morning trade saw iTraxx crossover start to widen out again, trading back up to 211bps. The weak German Ifo today weighed on stocks in Europe. The move, however, was seen as linked more to the general divergence of high and low grade credit sectors, with high beta single names having a particularly soft tone late morning.

Pearson and  General Electric said that they have decided against launching a counterbid for Dow Jones, clearing the way for News Corp to pursue the media company unchallenged. It was reported that GE had concluded it could not justify matching or beating News Corp's $5 bln offer and ruled out any other attempt to bid for Dow Jones.

It was reported in the FT, however, that Pearson and GE were discussing "co-operating agreements" between CNBC and the FT Group, which includes the Financial Times newspaper and FT.com and a
Page 12 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
controlling stake in  Interactive Data Corporation.

Separately, the WSJ said that  LVMH is in exclusive talks to buy the French financial daily Les Echos from Pearson. LVMH already owns French financial daily La Tribune so a purchase would make it the dominant
player in the country's financial news.

The market seemed to view the newsflow in a positive manner with shares in Pearson up 10.5p, trading at the 835p level, bucking the trend in the wider market. CDS spreads for Pearson were around 3 bps tighter this morning, trading at the 47 bps level.

Telefonica was in the news this morning following an article in the
FT that the group will next month be punished and fined by the European             Commission for abusing its dominant position in the market for broadband services. The antitrust regulators issued formal charges against the
group last year accusing it of operating a margin squeeze on competing groups.

The commission has yet to determine Telefonica's fine but under EU rules it can impose a penalty of up to 10% of the group's annual turnover. Shares in Telefonica were basically unchanged on the news this morning, trading at the E16.33 level, as were CDS spreads, trading in
the 25 bps area.

ArcelorMittal announced its European steel markets update this morning with the firm forecasting a "robust" market for flat products, adding that they ware also confident about demand for carbon steel.

The news that the firm has decided not to raise steel prices sent its shares almost 1% lower. The company is reportedly fixing prices to keep the market stable. Some analysts, however, had expected the strong demand in Europe to enable ArcelorMittal to possibly increase margins. Spreads were more stable on the report, however, trading marginally wider at 26bps.

--Kevin Hanly, Jeremy Spain, London newsroom: 00442076341627;
khanly@marketnews.com jspain@marketnews.com

HD           UPDATE 1-Spain's Telefonica  may face large EU fine
WC          509 words
PD           2 July 2007
ET           12:08 PM
SN           Reuters News
SC           LBA
LA           English
CY           (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Rewrites with sources on  Telefonica's  strategy)

MADRID, July 2 (Reuters) -  Telefonica  may face a large fine if the European Commission finds on Wednesday the dominant Spanish telecoms company did not give its competitors sufficient room to make a profit on the resale of ADSL Internet.

TD
Sources familiar with the Spanish company's thinking fear a  fine, and although they do not quantify how much that might be, newspaper El Pais reported on Monday it might run to as much as 100 million euros ($136 million).

The decision will be announced from Brussels on July 4 but until then the EU is expected to remain tight-lipped on the subject.

Telefonica  will appeal any decision which finds the company at fault, the sources said, as it would imply the group has been deliberately hindering its rivals.

The telecommunication company will also argue it has been following market rules on pricing as established by Spain's telecoms market commission CMT, and that the EU is effectively overruling the national watchdog.

"It is clear that the EU regulator does not trust the national ones ... That would be understandable if Telefonica  were a state telecom ... but it has been privately owned for some time now," one source familiar with the situation said.

Although the source understood the dispute as a technical rather than a political question, in order to reach its conclusion, the EU has recalculated  Telefonica's  accounts, disregarding data which the company believes is key.

"We hope the case will be closed without a  fine because we have always acted in good faith and according to the CMT's regulations," a spokesman for  Telefonica  said.

He added the market in Spanish ADSL fast Internet connections has developed well in recent years and that  Telefonica  has lost an increasing market share to its competitors.
According to CMT data, Spain had 5.76 million ADSL lines by the end of April, of which 4 million were
Telefonica's  retail lines while the rest, although on  Telefonica's  network, are operated by its rivals.
Broker BPI said if the news was confirmed, it would be a blow to  Telefonica's  image and its pocket. "(Payment of the  fine) would require up to 100 million euros of cashflow, equivalent to nearly 1 percent
of annual operational cash flow or 0.1 percent of its capitalisation," it said in a note.

EU antitrust regulators formally charged Spain's  Telefonica  early in 2006 with squeezing out smaller rivals who want to compete with high-speed Internet services, although the case dates back to 2002.

Telefonica  is the only company with country-wide fixed-line telephone facilities carrying high-speed
Internet over asymmetric digital subscriber lines (ADSL).

Page 14 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
With the exception of cable operators such as ONO, others who want to compete must buy some of
the ADSL service wholesale from  Telefonica  and re-sell it. (Additional reporting by Sabina Zawadzki in
Brussels)

MADRID (Dow Jones)--Spain's telecommunications market regulator, the CMT, said Tuesday it was unhappy with the  European Commission's investigation of an alleged margin squeeze  Telefonica  SA (TEF) has waged against its broadband competitors ahead of a ruling on the case expected Wednesday.
"We are far from thrilled about the E.U. watchdog's investigation, and would have preferred that the
CMT was doing it or at least was involved," the Chairman of the CMT, Reinaldo Rodriguez said.

TD
The E.U. last year issued formal charges against the  Telefonica,  accusing it of operating a margin squeeze on competitors.

The Brussels  antitrust watchdog found the wholesale cost of accessing  Telefonica's  broadband network was so close to prices charged to consumers that rivals were unable to earn a sufficient margin, effectively shutting them out of the market. [ 03-07-07 1313GMT ]

The commission has said the rollout of competitors broadband networks has been slower in Spain than in other European countries.
E.U. regulators can  fine companies up to 10% of their annual worldwide turnover, though the  fines
almost never reach that level.
People close to the situation have said  Telefonica  faces a fine of as much as EUR100 million. Company Web site: http://www.telefonica.com

HD       Telefonica  faces EU fine of close 100 mln euros
BY       JPM
WC     194 words
PD      3 July 2007
ET       05:42 PM
SN      Agence France Presse
SC      AFPR
LA       English
CY      Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2007 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, July 3, 2007 (AFP) -
Spanish operator Telefonica  faces a fine close to 100 million euros (136 million dollars) from EU
regulators for stifling competition in the broadband Internet market, a senior EU official said Tuesday.

TD
Several other officials told AFP that "the most probable" was that the  fine, expected Wednesday, would be "a bit more" than 100 million euros, although in theory it could reach 10 percent of annual sales.
In 2006, the company reported sales of nearly 53 billion euros.
In any case, the  fine will be the biggest that  competition regulators at the the  European Commission
have ever slapped on a European telecommunications operator.

In February, the Commission took the first step in legal action against  Telefonica,  levelling formal charges against the company in a "statement of objections."

The group has since had a chance to lay out its defence but seems to have had little success in convincing the Commission.

Regulators accuse Telefonica  of intentionally squeezing competitors in the market for broadband Internet access by the way it sets prices for rivals to use its network.

HD     Expansion: Spanish telecoms  regulator against Brussels plan to fine Telefonica  (La CMT carga contra Bruselas  ante la multimillonaria multa a Telefonica).
WC    187 words
PD     4 July 2007
SN     Expansión SC     EXPNAB LA      English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Expansion ©
2007 All rights reserved.

LP
Reinaldo Rodriguez, the chairman of the CMT, the Spanish telecommunications market commission, yesterday criticized the  European Commission's plan to  fine leading Spanish telecoms operator Telefonica  for abuse of dominant position in the internet broadband access market. The European authorities could today impose a  fine of over 100m euros on Telefonica  for having allegedly increased the wholesale prices charged to rival operators for using the group's infrastructure to the point that its competitors cannot make competitive offers, but ensuring that the profit margins are wide enough.

TD
The CMT, which authorized the offers now being penalized by Brussels, says that the wholesale prices charged by Telefonica  help to increase competition and enable the existence of alternative operators, adding that the planned  fine is not justified and could be detrimental to future investments in the sector. The CMT admits that ADSL prices in Spain are high, but it says that the funds help increase investments in internet infrastructure.
Original article by J.M. Abstracted from Expansion

HD       FT.com site : Telefonica  receives record fine. WC       284 words
PD        4 July 2007
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA        English
CY        (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
By Mark Mulligan in Madrid

The European Commission on Wednesday fined Telefonica  151.8m for market abuses, setting a new high in the telecommunications sector and realising the worst fears of Spain's biggest operator.

TD
The fine, which follows an investigation into the company's  abuse of its dominant position in Spain's broadband market, compares with sanctions of 10.35m and 12.6m in similar cases involving  Wanadoo of France and Germany's Deutsche Telecom in 2003.

Telefonica,  which last year reported group profits of 6.2bn, had hoped for a similarly lenient  fine. However, Brussels has toughened its stance on  antitrust infringements since Neelie Kroes took over as EU competition commissioner in 2004.

The Commission last year formally issued charges against  Telefonica  for operating a margin squeeze on competing operators since 2001. By charging excessive fees for access to its fixed-line network, the company stopped rivals from earning a sufficient margin on their broadband business. This had also stymied the development of competing networks and low-cost internet access, the Commission said on Wednesday.

"Spanish consumers are paying for more than the average for high-speed internet access and many have chosen not to pay that price," Ms Kroes said.

"The margin squeeze that  Telefonica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs, but harmed customers significantly.
"When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers. "I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation," she said.

Telefonica,  which has always denied the charges, is almost certain to appeal to the European Court of
First Instance, the EU's second-highest court.

Page 22 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD       FT.com site : Telefonica  hit by 152m antitrust fine. BY        Tobias Buck in Brussels and Mark Mulligan in Madrid
WC      489 words
PD       4 July 2007
SN       Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC       FTCOM
LA        English
CY       (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Telefonica  was told on Wednesday to pay a 152m ($207m)  antitrust fine, after the  European               Commission found Spain's dominant telecoms group guilty of illegally freezing out rivals from the lucrative broadband market.

It is the largest  fine Brussels has ever imposed on a telecoms company, outstripping earlier penalties imposed on Deutsche Telekom and France's  Wanadoo for similar abuses by a factor of 10. With the exception of Microsoft, no other company has been punished more severely for abusing a dominant position.

TD
The EU's top  antitrust regulator said the fine was intended as a warning to other groups, and reflected Brussels' determination to defend competition in newly liberalised markets such as energy and telecoms.  Telefonica  rejected the charges, and vowed to appeal against the ruling.

"Telefonica's  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy," said Neelie Kroes, the EU  competition commissioner. "I want to send a strong signal to dominant undertakings in all sectors that could be tempted to engage in similar practices that I will not tolerate such behaviour," she added.

The Commission found  Telefonica  operated a "margin squeeze" on its rivals for more than five years beginning in September 2001. The group weakened its competitors by setting the wholesale cost of accessing Telefonica's  broadband network so close to the retail price that rivals were forced to make losses to stay in the market,Brussels said.

Ms Kroes said the group's behaviour had undermined the growth of broadband in one of Europe's largest economies: "Spain has a broadband penetration rate well below the EU average. Consumers have been put off from exploring the new services that broadband internet allows."

Telefonica  yesterday railed against the size of the  fine, describing it as "unjustified and disproportionate".

The Commission's case against  Telefonica  has also raised questions about the regulation of Spain's broadband segment, where network access charges are fixed by the Telecoms Market Commission (CMT). The group has repeatedly pointed to the CMT's approval of its rates in its defence against Brussels, exposing an embarrassing rift between the Spanish regulator and the Commission.

Telefonica  said it had always "complied scrupulously with the rules imposed by the CMT". It added: "We believe that the  fine punishes us for being caught between two sets of contradictory rules - those of the CMT and those of the European regulator." Until late 2003, retail charges were set by the Spanish government, and the CMT used these to calculate wholesale charges which were supposed to allow space for competitors to make money in the broadband market.

When retail prices were liberalised, it began calculating the wholesale tariffs according to real market prices provided by Telefonica.
The company said it would present its appeal "within the next few days".
RF        57142862

HD       FT.com site : European Central Bank turns a blind eye to sniping. BY       Paul Betts
WC      801 words
PD       4 July 2007
SN       Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC       FTCOM
LA       English
CY       (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
If in doubt, do like the Bundesbank. This seems to have become the prevailing creed at the European Central Bank these days. Just like the old German central bank, the ECB is defending its independent role in fixing European interest rates and studiously ignoring the continual sniping from the sidelines over its monetary tightening.

The strength of the euro and the ECB's current rate tightening cycle is being increasingly blamed by some countries - with France in the vanguard - for holding back growth and undermining export competitiveness. True, many companies complain that the strong euro is making life difficult.
Peugeot-Citroen's new boss, Christian Streiff, claims the single currency is about 30 per cent overvalued against the Japanese yen, which is at an all-time low against the euro. Airbus keeps whingeing that it is being heavily penalised by the unfavourable euro-dollar exchange.

TD
But how do these critics explain the continuing strength of German exports or indeed Germany's impressive export performance back in the old days of the strong Deutschmark?

How do they respond to the report the  European Commission released this week arguing that the rise of the euro had taken only "a modest toll" so far on export growth in the eurozone?

The problem seems to be elsewhere. Could it be that the re-energised German economy is now creating an imbalance in Europe with neighbouring countries struggling to match German performance?

In the past, countries such as Italy, Spain or France have been able to take advantage of a benign interest rate climate while Germany was struggling with reunification. Unfortunately, these countries did not seize the opportunity of this favourable economic climate to restructure their own houses. Germany, on the other hand, was forced to and is now reaping the fruits of all its hard work: wage restraint and corporate restructuring.

In a single currency system, there are fundamentally only two solutions to restore the balance. Germany's neighbours must either reform or Germany itself could allow its European partners a bit more breathing space by tolerating to live with higher inflation for a while.
But the Berlin government is unlikely to buy this and nor should it.

Monetary laxity has rarely delivered sustainable growth and it certainly does not encourage countries to adopt the financial and budgetary disciplines necessary for longer-term prosperity.
Sure, many of Germany's neighbours probably need more time to put together credible internal reforms. The ECB is also clearly aware of the current tensions and could be more accommodating.
But it will only do so if it is convinced that governments are serious in carrying out their reform attempts. It obviously does not consider that the evidence to date is sufficient.
Best of enemies

The European Commission's decision yesterday to make an example of  Telefonica  for abuses of its dominant position in the broadband market is a fairly serious blow for the Spanish telecoms group.
Page 25 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Although the company is big enough to absorb the hefty 152m ($207m)  fine - assuming its appeal fails - the sanction undermines a concerted campaign to shed its image in Spain as the big, bad
market-abusing incumbent.

Competitors and consumers readily agree that, in some cases, the company's marketing and pricing techniques are questionable at best and, yes, abusive at worst. Ironically, however, in the case of network access charges to broadband competitors,  Telefonica  appears to have been playing by the rules.

Executives have always maintained their innocence and those from some rival groups admit privately that Brussels may have come down a little hard on the incumbent.

Spain's Telecoms Market Commission (CMT), the market regulator, has been particularly critical of the sanction, arguing that, as it sets wholesale tariffs for fixed-line services, the scope for  abuse is minimal. When the tariff system was introduced in 2001, it argues,  Telefonica  enjoyed a whopping 95 per cent of the fixed-line market. This has since been pared to 55 per cent.

Although conceding that the country's broadband market has been slow to evolve, its system for calculating network access charges - based on subtracting from the final cost to consumers - is not the culprit, it argues.

The fact that the CMT has taken offence at the EC's charges against  Telefonica  is not surprising: the sanction is akin to a slap in the face for the regulator.

But it has given rise to a most surprising alliance. In normal circumstances, the CMT and  Telefonica  are at each other's throats, the former disallowing unfairly priced products and the latter complaining of overzealousness. Suddenly, they are comrades in arms. Whatever  Telefonica  and the CMT say about the EC, they may one day thank it for bringing them together.

European antitrust regulators will fineTelefonica SA as much as €150-million ($204-million U.S.) for abusing its dominance in broadband Internet access in Spain, two people with direct knowledge of the case said. 1.30pm:  European commission finds O2 owner guilty of squeezing rival operators out of the
Spanish broadband internet market. By David Gow in Brussels.
Neelie Kroes, EU  competition commissioner, today  fined Telefónica  a record &#8364;152m
(£103m) for squeezing rival operators out of the Spanish broadband internet market.

TD
The European commission found that the Spanish telecoms group, which owns O2, had charged its rivals too much for wholesale broadband services, compared to the cost of its own high-speed
internet product.

Ms Kroes coupled the  fine with a warning to energy groups and other incumbent companies accused of abusing their dominance that she would not hesitate to impose similarly swingeing penalties.
"The sky is not the limit," she said.

In a frontal attack on the protectionist policies of new French president Nicolas Sarkozy, she repeatedly proclaimed that the commission's anti-trust authorities are the real protectors of citizen and consumer interests by defending them against dominant groups. "I will not tolerate such behaviour," she said.

She added: "Telefónica's  conduct (over more than five years) harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy ...
"The fact is that small businesses and consumers in Spain are paying about 20% more than the
EU-15 average for high-speed (ADSL) internet access."

The fine, second only to the &#8364;497m imposed on Microsoft for abusing its dominant position, is a record for the telecoms sector and 10 times higher than those imposed in 2003 on Deutsche Telekom and France Télécom's internet arm  Wanadoo. Ms Kroes said these had failed to act as a deterrent.

"Telefónica  could have ended this abuse. It did not do so," she told reporters. The commission began inquiries into the Telefónica  case in late 2003 after Wanadoo's Spanish arm launched a formal complaint, and opened formal proceedings in February 2006. Wanadoo later became Orange broadband.

But the Spanish regulator, forewarned of the EC's move, did not act until December last year to curb Telefónica.  The Spanish telecoms group, Ms Kroes said, blamed the regulator and had lobbied intensively in the media and in Brussels against the pending  fine.

Spanish observers accused Ms Kroes of undermining the regulator's authority by intervening but she dismissed these charges. The regulator is seen in Brussels as pliable to pressure from governments of both centre-right and centre-left hue - and from  Telefónica  itself.

The Spanish telco said it would immediately lodge an appeal with the European court of justice. It insisted it had abided with the regulator's guidelines.

HD          UPDATE 3-EU fines Telefonica  record 151.9 mln euros
BY          By Sabina Zawadzki
WC         714 words
PD          4 July 2007
ET           11:29 AM
SN          Reuters News
SC          LBA
LA           English
CY          (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
(Adds Telefonica,  Orange statement)

BRUSSELS, July 4 (Reuters) - The European Union's top  antitrust authority  fined Spanish telecoms firm Telefonica  a record 151.9 million euros ($207 million) for squeezing out rivals from the high-speed Internet market.

TD
The European Commission said Telefonica's  wholesale prices to other Internet providers were too high compared with its own retail prices, a margin squeeze that meant rivals made losses when they resold the access to consumers.

"Telefonica's  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy,"  Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes told reporters.

"I want to send a strong signal to dominant undertakings in all sectors that could be tempted to engage in similar practices that I will not tolerate such behaviour," she said, referring to the energy market, dominated by former state monopolies.

Telefonica  said it would immediately appeal the "unjustified and disproportionate" decision, arguing it was following market rules on pricing as set by Spain's telecoms market commission CMT, and that the EU was overruling the national watchdog.

The fine is the highest set against a telecoms firm and the second-highest the European Union's top antitrust regulator has levied in an "abuse of dominance" case, beaten by almost half a billion euros Microsoft had to pay in 2004.

CMT, which had dropped a similar case against  Telefonica,  on Tuesday criticised the Commission for stepping on its turf, adding that the EU executive should start a case against CMT if it was unhappy with the national telecoms regulator's actions.
Shares in the company were little changed, trading 0.4 percent up at 16.51 euros by 1141 GMT.

"The fine sets a precedent in terms of size although it's small change for  Telefonica,  but the decision does seem a little unusual," said a telecoms analyst at one London investment bank.
The EU complaint was orginally filed by  Wanadoo, an Internet service belonging to France Telecom's
Orange.

"The EU's decision confirms the arguments used that Orange made against the serious issues affecting  competition in the Spanish ADSL market," Orange said in statement.

TELEFONICA AWARE

The Commission said CMT's pricing was based on  Telefonica's  forecasts, which "were not matched by its actual data".

Page 30 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze," the EU executive said.
It added CMT regulated only  Telefonica's  regional wholesale prices -- 30 percent of its prices.
CMT significantly lowered the prices after the Commission opened its formal investigation in February
2006. The  fine covers the period between 2001 and 2006.
Analysts backed  Telefonica's  arguments that it was merely following CMT's guidelines.

"What Brussels should be looking at is whether the national regulator has favoured  Telefonica...  This decision will have consequences...it doesn't make any sense," said Nahum Sanchez of brokerage Caja Madrid Bolsa.

Telefonica  is the only company in Spain with country-wide fixed-line telephone facilities carrying high-speed Internet over asymmetric digital subscriber lines (ADSL).
With the exception of cable operators such as ONO, others wanting to compete must buy some of the
ADSL service wholesale from  Telefonica  and resell it.

According to CMT data, Spain had 5.76 million ADSL lines by the end of April. Four million of those were Telefonica's  retail lines while the rest, although on  Telefonica's  network, were operated by its rivals.

The Commission said Spanish consumers paid 20 percent more than the average of the 15 "old" members of the EU, while broadband penetration was 20 percent below the EU-15's average.

Kroes' warning shot at other sectors was aimed chiefly at dominant energy companies which control both the supply of energy and the network through which it is transported.

Competitors often need to buy access to this network if they want to supply their own consumers. Kroes has already opened  antitrust investigations into Germany's RWE and E.ON and wants firms to spin off their networks. (Additional reporting by Elisabeth O'Leary and Robert Hetz in Madrid)

[F/AU=REASC F/UI=JAL,ASC F/ED=EDJAL F/UN=LEECHJ

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission Wednesday fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA (TEF) EUR151.9 million, saying the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rivals in the broadband business.

Telefonica  squeezed its competitors' margins by raising their fees for access to the telephone network and lowering its own prices for retail broadband Internet services, the commission said in a statement. [
07-04-07 0530ET ]
About 80% of Spain's high-speed Internet connections are made over fixed telephone lines.

Telefonica,  a state-owned monopoly until 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with a nationwide fixed-line network. This enabled the company to rig the country's broadband market, the commission said, noting that between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between Telefonica's  retail prices and the prices for wholesale broadband access were too thin for other providers to cover their costs.

"The margin squeeze that  Telefonica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs, but also harmed customers significantly. When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," the commission's top  antitrust official, Neelie Kroes, said in a statement.

The commission's penalty is part of a broader effort to shake up Europe's telecommunications sector, which is still dominated by former state-owned monopolies. Over the past year, the regulator has pressed operators to lower mobile phone roaming charges.

The commission last week took Germany to court for giving Deutsche Telekom AG (DT) a legal monopoly over the country's broadband network. [ 07-04-07 0533ET ]

Telefonica's  fine also shows that the commission is escalating its crackdown on antitrust abuses. In a similar case in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom (FTE), was fined only EUR10.4 million. In a range of recent cartel cases,  fines have been several times larger than in similar cases just a few years ago.

Fining Wanadoo helped pry open the French broadband market, the commission said, adding that the sector is now "among the most competitive in Europe, with lower prices" and a growing number of users.

In Spain, by contrast, broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest E.U. member countries. Spain's broadband penetration rate is also 20% lower than average, according to
the commission.

Page 33 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze, the commission said.

The commission opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February 2006. That prompted Spanish regulators to force the company to lower its wholesale prices in December
2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the commission.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com [ 07-04-07
0558ET ]

HD        UPDATE:EU Fines Telefonica  EUR151.9M For Broadband Mkt Abuses

(Updates an item that ran at 0945 GMT with company response, commission comments, further details)

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission Wednesday fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA (TEF) EUR151.9 million, saying the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rival broadband providers.

TD
Telefonica  squeezed its competitors' margins by raising fees for access to its telephone network and lowering its own prices for retail broadband services, the commission said in a statement.

Telefonica  was "making it virtually impossible for competitors to enter the market," the commission's top
antitrust official, Neelie Kroes, told a news conference.

The commission's penalty, its highest ever against a telecommunications company, is part of a broader effort to shake up the sector, which is still dominated by former state-owned monopolies. Over the past year, the regulator has pressed operators to lower mobile phone roaming charges and has pushed for changes in several broadband markets. The commission last week took Germany to court for giving Deutsche Telekom AG (DT) a legal monopoly over the country's broadband network.

Telefonica's  fine shows how the regulator is escalating its crackdown on  antitrust abuses by levying heftier fines. In similar cases in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom (FTE), was  fined EUR10.4 million and Deutsche Telekom was penalized EUR12.6 million. In a range of recent collusion cases, fines have been several times larger than in comparable cases just a few years ago.

Kroes said Telefonica's  fine was justified because of the seriousness of the  abuse and the harm done to consumers. Spanish broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest E.U. member countries and Spain's broadband penetration rate is 20% lower than average, according to the commission.

Telefonica  said it will appeal the  fine, calling the commission's penalty "unjustified and disproportionate." It added that it has complied with rules established by Spain's telecommunications regulator.

"Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators, the national regulator and the  European       Commission, which are at odds with each other," the company said in a statement.

Telefonica  said its share of Spain's broadband market has decreased steadily to its current 55% from
95% in 2001. The company's main competitors for broadband services include Orange, a unit of France
Telecom, and Jazztel PLC (JAZ.MC).

Being a former state-owned monopoly helped  Telefonica  rig Spain's broadband market, according to the commission. About 80% of high-speed Internet connections in Spain are made over fixed telephone lines. Telefonica,  which was privatized in 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with a nationwide fixed-line network.

The commission said that between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between Telefonica's  retail prices and the fees it charged for network access was too thin for other providers to cover their costs.


Page 35 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company "could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze," the commission said.

The E.U. regulator opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February 2006. That prompted Spain's telecommunications authority to force the company to lower its wholesale prices in December 2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the commission.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com
(Peppi Kiviniemi and Jason Sinclair contributed to this article) [ 07-04-07 0756ET ]

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission Wednesday fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA (TEF) EUR151.9 million, saying the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rival broadband providers.

The commission said that between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between Telefonica's  retail prices and the fees it charged for network access was too thin for other providers to cover their costs.

Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company "could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze," the commission said.
The E.U. regulator opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February
2006. That prompted Spain's telecommunications authority to force the company to lower its
wholesale prices in December 2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the
commission.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com
(Peppi Kiviniemi and Jason Sinclair contributed to this article) [ 07-04-07 0756ET ]

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission Wednesday fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA (TEF) EUR151.9 million, saying the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rival broadband providers.

TD
Telefonica  squeezed its competitors' margins by raising fees for access to its telephone network and lowering its own prices for retail broadband services, the commission said in a statement.

Telefonica  was "making it virtually impossible for competitors to enter the market," the commission's top antitrust official, Neelie Kroes, told a news conference.

The commission's penalty, its highest ever against a telecommunications company, is part of a broader effort to shake up the sector, which is still dominated by former state-owned monopolies. Over the past year, the regulator has pressed operators to lower mobile phone roaming charges and has pushed for changes in several broadband markets. The commission last week took Germany to court for giving Deutsche Telekom AG (DT) a legal monopoly over the country's broadband network.

Telefonica's  fine shows how the regulator is escalating its crackdown on  antitrust abuses by levying heftier fines. In similar cases in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom (FTE), was fined EUR10.4 million and Deutsche Telekom was penalized EUR12.6 million. In a range of recent collusion cases,  fines have been several times larger than in comparable cases just a few years ago.

Kroes said Telefonica's  fine was justified because of the seriousness of the  abuse and the harm done to consumers. Spanish broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest E.U. member countries and Spain's broadband penetration rate is 20% lower than average, according to the commission.

Telefonica  said it will appeal the  fine, calling the commission's penalty "unjustified and disproportionate." It added that it has complied with rules established by Spain's telecommunications regulator.

"Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators, the national regulator and the  European  Commission, which are at odds with each other," the company said in a statement.

Telefonica  said its share of Spain's broadband market has decreased steadily to its current 55%
from 95% in 2001. The company's main competitors for broadband services include Orange, a unit of
France Telecom, and Jazztel PLC (JAZ.MC). Orange first complained to the commission about
Telefonica's  business practices in 2003.
The commission's  fine "confirms the validity of Orange's (complaint)," Orange said in a statement. A
spokesman for Jazztel declined to comment.

Being a former state-owned monopoly helped  Telefonica  rig Spain's broadband market, according to the commission. About 80% of high-speed Internet connections in Spain are made over fixed


Page 43 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
telephone lines.  Telefonica,  which was privatized in 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with a nationwide fixed-line network.

Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company "could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze," the E.U. regulator said. Between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between  Telefonica's  retail prices and the fees it charged for network access was too thin for other providers to cover their costs, the commission added.
The commission opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February
2006. That prompted Spain's telecommunications authority to force the company to lower its
wholesale prices in December 2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the
commission.

BRUSSELS (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission Wednesday fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA (TEF) EUR151.9 million, saying the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rivals in the broadband business.

Telefonica  squeezed its competitors' margins by raising their fees for access to the telephone network and lowering its own prices for retail broadband Internet services, the commission said in a statement. [
04-07-07 0930GMT ]
About 80% of Spain's high-speed Internet connections are made over fixed telephone lines.

Telefonica,  a state-owned monopoly until 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with a nationwide fixed-line network. This enabled the company to rig the country's broadband market, the commission said, noting that between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between Telefonica's  retail prices and the prices for wholesale broadband access were too thin for other providers to cover their costs.

"The margin squeeze that  Telefonica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs, but also harmed customers significantly. When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," the commission's top  antitrust official, Neelie Kroes, said in a statement.

The commission's penalty is part of a broader effort to shake up Europe's telecommunications sector, which is still dominated by former state-owned monopolies. Over the past year, the regulator has pressed operators to lower mobile phone roaming charges.

The commission last week took Germany to court for giving Deutsche Telekom AG (DT) a legal monopoly over the country's broadband network. [ 04-07-07 0933GMT ]

Telefonica's  fine also shows that the commission is escalating its crackdown on antitrust abuses. In a similar case in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom (FTE), was fined only EUR10.4 million. In a range of recent cartel cases,  fines have been several times larger than in similar cases just a few years ago.

Fining Wanadoo helped pry open the French broadband market, the commission said, adding that the sector is now "among the most competitive in Europe, with lower prices" and a growing number of users.

In Spain, by contrast, broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest E.U. member countries. Spain's broadband penetration rate is also 20% lower than average, according to
the commission.

Page 45 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze, the commission said.

The commission opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February 2006. That prompted Spanish regulators to force the company to lower its wholesale prices in December
2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the commission.

TOP STORIES

BLACKSTONE ADDS TO HILTON HOLDINGS WITH $18.5B HILTON BUY
Private equity firm Blackstone makes a big move into the hotel sector with an $18.5 billion deal to buy
Hilton Hotels. The deal for $47.50 a share in cash plus the assumption of $7.5 billion in debt made at a
40% premium over Monday's $33.87 closing price for the prominent hotel group. Hotel shares are up
across Europe in reaction to the deal.

TD



PRIVATE EQUITY HEAVYWEIGHT KKR FILES FOR $1.25B IPO
Private-equity heavyweight Kohlberg Kravis Roberts files for an initial public offering, signaling a broad power shift on Wall Street that poses head-on  competition to the Street's biggest banks.



UK'S DARLING WARY OF BUY-OUT TAX RISE
Chancellor of the Exchequer Alistair Darling rules out an immediate clampdown on private equity's tax privileges, fearing undesirable effects on the "critical" role the City plays in the economy, the Financial Times reports.



EU
FINES TELEFONICA FOR BROADBAND ABUSES
The European Commission fines Spain's  Telefonica  EUR151.9 million, saying it used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rivals in the broadband business by raising their access fees to the telephone network.



GUNMEN ATTACK NIGERIAN OIL INSTALLATION
Gunmen attacked an oil installation today in Nigeria's restive southern oil heartland and seized five expatriate workers, private security officials say. Police are investigating a reported incident, but have no details yet.

U.K. reporter Alan Johnston is released after nearly four months in captivity in the Gaza Strip following mediation efforts by Hamas to secure his freedom. Johnston says it was "fantastic" to be free after an "appalling" ordeal.

MOSQUE LEADERS DEFIANT AS DEADLINE TO SURRENDER PASSES
Pakistan's government give Islamic militants holed up inside a besieged mosque in the capital an imminent deadline to surrender, a day after clashes there killed at least nine people.



TOKYO SHARES END UP FOR 5TH SESSION
The Nikkei 225 Stock Average closes marginally higher for its fifth straight session of gains with real estate stocks among the most prominent gainers on a report that Cerberus Capital Management is to sell its Imperial Hotel stake.



MOODY'S SETS COURSE FOR JAPAN UPGRADE
Moody's Investors Service gives its seal of approval to Japan's efforts to rein in its massive debt, placing on review for possible upgrade the Japanese government's A2 rating for domestic securities. Yen briefly rallies on the news.



FRENCH FINANCE MINISTER TO 'WATCH' DANONE DEAL
The French Finance Ministry will "pay close attention" to the acquisition of Groupe Danone's biscuit division to Kraft Foods, even though deal is "a private transaction" Finance minister Christine Lagarde tells iTele TV station.



UK HOUSE PRICE GROWTH SLOWS ON RATE HIKES
U.K. house prices picked up a little in June, although a 2.0% rise in the second quarter was the weakest gain since the third quarter of 2006, showing that the Bank of England's four rate rises since August last year are taking their toll.

HD        UPDATE: EU Fines Telefonica  EUR151.9M, Tightening Mkt Grip
BY         By Jason Sinclair
CR        Of DOW JONES NEWSWIRES
WC        433 words
PD         4 July 2007
ET         12:10 PM
SN         Dow Jones International News
SC         DJI
LA         English
CY         (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
MADRID (Dow Jones)--The  European Commission slapped a hefty  fine on Telefonica  SA (TEF) Wednesday for abusing its dominant position in the broadband market, as the EU watchdog rachets up its control on the European telecoms sector.

The largest telecommunications company in Spain and Latin America was  fined EUR151.9 million for operating a margin squeeze on its high-speed Internet competitors since 2001. This is the biggest  fine against a telecommunications company ever imposed by the Commission.

TD
The Brussels  antitrust watchdog found the wholesale cost of accessing  Telefonica's  broadband network was so close to prices charged to consumers that rivals were unable to earn a sufficient margin, shutting them out of the market. The commission also argued the rollout of competitors broadband networks has been slower in Spain than in other European countries due to  Telefonica's practices.

Telefonica  balked at what it said were conflicting criteria between the EU regulator and the Spanish telecom regulator, the CMT.

"The CMT hasn't ruled against us for these reasons, and it seems strange that after years of accepting our prices and practices, now a different regulator is saying there was wrongdoing," a company spokesman said.
At 0956 GMT,  Telefonica  shares were 0.5% higher at EUR16.52 in an overall positive Madrid market. "Telefonica  has a market capitalization of EUR79 billion and the impact of this  fine is minimal, what
could be more significant is if  Telefonica  is now facing a more restrictive regulatory scrutiny from
Brussels," UBS analyst Bosco Ojeda said. UBS rates  Telefonica  at neutral with a EUR17.70 price
target.

The hefty  fine also signals a departure from the EU's former soft-glove approach to regulating European telecommunications companies. In 2003, two other telecommunications incumbents, France Telecom (FTE) and Deutsche Telekom AG (DT) were  fined EUR10.4 million and EUR12.6 million respectively for similar infractions. But since Neelie Kroes took the helm as EU Competition Commissioner in 2004, the EU has tightened its grip on  antitrust infractions, and hiked its fines.

"Brussels is trying to make an example out of the  Telefonica  case and send a message to the European national regulators," Juan Tuesta from local brokerage Banesto said. "It's clear the EU is trying to wrestle control from domestic regulators, what remains to be seen is how far they are going to go," Tuesta added.
Company Web site: http://www.telefonica.com
-By Jason Sinclair, Dow Jones Newswires, 34 913958127, jason.sinclair@dowjones.com [ 04-07-07
1010GMT ]
CO        comtn : Telefonica SA

In similar cases in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom (FTE), was  fined EUR10.4 million and Deutsche Telekom was penalized EUR12.6 million. In a range of recent collusion cases, fines have been several times larger than in comparable cases just a few years ago.

Kroes said Telefonica's  fine was justified because of the seriousness of the  abuse and the harm done to consumers. Spanish broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest E.U. member countries and Spain's broadband penetration rate is 20% lower than average, according to the commission.

Telefonica  said it will appeal the  fine, calling the commission's penalty "unjustified and disproportionate." It added that it has complied with rules established by Spain's telecommunications regulator.

"Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators, the national regulator and the  European       Commission, which are at odds with each other," the company said in a statement.

Telefonica  said its share of Spain's broadband market has decreased steadily to its current 55% from
95% in 2001. The company's main competitors for broadband services include Orange, a unit of France
Telecom, and Jazztel PLC (JAZ.MC). Orange first complained to the commission about  Telefonica's
business practices in 2003.
The commission's  fine "confirms the validity of Orange's (complaint)," Orange said in a statement. A
spokesman for Jazztel declined to comment.

Being a former state-owned monopoly helped  Telefonica  rig Spain's broadband market, according to the commission. About 80% of high-speed Internet connections in Spain are made over fixed telephone lines. Telefonica,  which was privatized in 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with
a nationwide fixed-line network.


Page 57 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts show that the company "could not have been unaware that it was engaging in a margin squeeze," the E.U. regulator said. Between September 2001 and December 2006, the margin between  Telefonica's  retail prices and the fees it charged for network access was too thin for other providers to cover their costs, the commission added.

The commission opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in February 2006. That prompted Spain's telecommunications authority to force the company to lower its wholesale prices in December 2006, ending the company's  antitrust abuses, according to the commission.
-By Adam Cohen, Dow Jones Newswires; +322 741 1486; adam.cohen@dowjones.com
(Peppi Kiviniemi and Jason Sinclair contributed to this article) [ 04-07-07 1226GMT ]

BRUSSELS, July 4 (Xinhua) -- The  European Commission on Wednesday announced a
151-million-euro antitrust fine against Spanish telecoms operator  Telefonica.

The hefty  fine was imposed since Telefonica  committed a "very serious  abuse" of its dominant position in the Spanish broadband market, the European Union's  antitrust watchdog said.

TD
The Commission found that from Sept. 2001 to Dec. 2006, the Spanish telecoms giant structured its wholesale and retail broadband prices at both the national and regional levels in such a way that the margin between them did not allow rivals to compete on the market without making losses.

"Through this so-called 'margin squeeze,'  Telefonica  insulated itself from the rigors of  competition by making it impossible for alternative broadband suppliers to enter the market on a commercially-viable basis," said Neelie Kroes, European Commissioner for  Competition Policy.

As a consequence of  Telefonica's  unfair practice, small businesses and consumers in Spain are paying about 20 percent more than the average for high-speed Internet access in the old 15 EU member
states, the Commission claimed.

HD         2nd LD: EU fines Spanish telecoms  operator  Telefonica
WC        313 words
PD         4 July 2007
SN         Xinhua News Agency
SC         XNEWS

CY         (c) Copyright 2007 Xinhua News Agency

BRUSSELS, July 4 (Xinhua) -- The European Commission on Wednesday announced an antitrust   
fine of over 151 million euros against Spanish telecoms operator  Telefonica.

The hefty  fine was imposed because Telefonica  had committed a "very serious  abuse" of its dominant position in the Spanish broadband market, the European Union's  antitrust watchdog said.

TD
The Commission found that from Sept. 2001 to Dec. 2006, the Spanish telecom giant structured its wholesale and retail broadband prices at both the national and regional levels in such a way that the margin between them did not allow rivals to compete without making a loss.

"Through this so-called 'margin squeeze,'  Telefonica  insulated itself from the rigors of  competition by making it impossible for alternative broadband suppliers to enter the market on a commercially-viable basis," said Neelie Kroes, European Commissioner for  Competition Policy.

Telefonica's  rivals had to buy wholesale broadband access from it because most broadband connections in Spain are provided through fixed telephone lines, with the only nation-wide network controlled by Telefonica.

By setting a high wholesale price,  Telefonica  made its rivals unable to make profit if they wanted to provide lower retail prices.
As a consequence of  Telefonica's  unfair practice, small businesses and consumers in Spain paid about
20 percent more than the average for high-speed Internet access in the old 15 EU member states, the
Commission claimed.

The fine is the biggest ever slapped on a European telecoms operator and the second biggest the commission has imposed for  abuse of market dominance after the U.S. software giant Microsoft was hit with a penalty of nearly half a billion euros in 2004.

"I want to send a strong signal to dominant undertakings in all sectors that could be tempted to engage in similar practices that I will not tolerate such behavior," Kroes said.

BRUSSELS, July 4, 2007 (AFP) -

EU antitrust regulators levelled a 151-million-euro  fine on Spanish operator  Telefonica  Wednesday in the biggest competition penalty ever slapped on a European telecommunications group.

TD
The European Commission said it had imposed the  fine, worth 206 million dollars, because  Telefonica
had stifled competition in the market for broadband Internet market.

"Spanish consumers are paying far more than the average for high-speed Internet access and many have chosen not to pay that price," EU  Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

Europe's top  antitrust watchdog accused Telefonica  of "a very serious  abuse of its dominant position" by squeezing rival broadband access providers' margins for over five years in the way it set wholesale and retail prices.

"In so doing,  Telefonica  weakened its competitors, making their continued presence and growth difficult: competitors were forced to make losses if they wanted to match  Telefonica's  retail prices," the Commission said in a statement.
Kroes said that the arrangement made Spanish consumers pay 20 percent more than the average in the
European Union's oldest member states, stunting the market's growth.

"The margin squeeze that  Telefonica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs, but also harmed customers significantly," Kroes said.

"When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," she added.

HD      Spanish telecom Telefonica  will appeal hefty EU fine
BY      FZ

MADRID, July 4, 2007 (AFP) -
Spanish telecommunications operator  Telefonica  vowed Wedenesday to challenge a 151.9-million-euro
fine imposed on it by EU  antitrust regulators in a campaign against uncompetitive practices.

TD
Telefonica  described the EU ruling as "inexplicable" and said it would appeal the finding at the European
Court of Justice in Luxembourg.

European Union Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said she was imposing the  fine, worth 206 million dollars, because Telefonica  had stifled  competition in the broadband Internet market.

Kroes said she wanted to "send a strong signal" to other companies that crush rivals unfairly using dominant market power, especially in key sectors such as telecommunications, energy and transport.

The fine is the largest ever slapped on a telecoms operator in Europe and the second largest the Commission has imposed for abusing market strength after a hitting Microsoft with a penalty of nearly half a billion euros in 2004.

"Telefonica  finds the decision by Brussels to be unjustified and disproportionate, both legally and economically," the company said in a statement.

It insisted that it had "scrupulously respected" the conditions demanded by the Spanish regulatory authority and said it had been caught between two contradictory sets of norms, one imposed by the Spanish regulator and another by the  European Commission.
The EU decision, it added, creates "legal uncertainty that will inevitably affect the future plans of
Telefonica  and (other) operators."

HD     EU fines Spain's Telefonica  more than €151 million for unfair Internet prices

BY      By AOIFE WHITE CR     AP Business Writer WC     719 words
PD      4 July 2007
ET      03:26 PM
SN      Associated Press Newswires
SC      APRS
LA      English
CY      (c) 2007. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS, Belgium (AP) - EU regulators  fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA €151.9 million (US$206.5 million) on Wednesday, claiming the company unfairly squeezed rivals by setting wholesale Internet prices too high to allow them turn a profit.

EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said the high fine -- second only in EU monopoly  abuse penalties to the landmark €497 million (US$613 million) fine on Microsoft Corp. -- reflected the fact that Telefonica  knew it was breaking EU  antitrust rules.

TD
"Telefonica's  price structures raised its competitors' costs, restricted  competition on the retail market, and made consumers pay the price," she told reporters, saying the decision was a warning to others in newly opened sectors -- such as energy.

The company said it would appeal the EU decision it called "unjustified and disproportionate," and said it contradicted ongoing supervision from the Spanish telecom regulator.

It said this created legal uncertainty about which regulator was in charge, which would inevitably affect its ability to launch new products and services.
"Telefonica  has legitimate reasons to believe it has strictly adhered to the regulations governing fair
competition and has acted in good faith," it said.

Spain's former national monopoly controls the fixed line telephone network used for 80 percent of all ADSL broadband Internet connections. Rivals need to buy wholesale access from  Telefonica  to offer competing retail services.

The European Commission said these wholesale prices on regional and national networks were set so high from 2001 to 2006 that they held back the rollout of broadband Internet in Spain. As a result, the EU said consumers there use the Internet less and pay a fifth more than other western Europeans.

"When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," Kroes said. "Consumers have been put off from exploring the new services that broadband Internet allows."

The fine is far higher than other  antitrust fines on telecom companies in similar cases, such as a €12.6 million (US$17 million) penalty on Deutsche Telekom AG or a €10 million (US$13 million)  fine against France Telecom AG's internet arm  Wanadoo.

Kroes said these  fines had not been big enough if they had failed to deter  Telefonica  from also trying to cripple rivals.

"We have had to intervene because the incumbents still control the fixed local access networks built when they had exclusive rights, and this control gives them the ability and the incentive to exclude competitors from related markets, such as broadband Internet access," she said.


Page 67 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
She said she would not tolerate similar behavior in other newly liberalized markets, just days after the EU electricity sector fully opened up. The EU is investigating  antitrust abuse by European gas companies and has signaled it may open further probes.
"I will not allow dominant companies to use their market power to close down markets that the European
Union has opened," she said.

Regulators said Telefonica's  business plan and cost accounts showed that the company would have known that margins were too low to be cover costs. They said  Telefonica  is and was profitable along its entire supply chain but has been losing money in the retail market.

But Telefonica  had argued that the Commission had not proved that competitors were losing money, claiming no companies had been forced to exit the market and several had entered.

"The Commission's line of argument is totally contradictory, since high retail prices by definition provide competitors with a margin," it said.
Broadband has grown rapidly in Spain in recent years, it said, and  competition has increased with
Telefonica's  market share falling from 95 percent in 2001 to the current 55 percent.

Although the Spanish regulatory agency, the CMT, had cleared  Telefonica's  regional wholesale prices before it set a cap in 2006, the EU said it had used market and cost forecasts -- instead of the EU's historical data -- and had not looked into the margin squeeze problem or national network access.

"There is nothing extraordinary or exceptional in the fact that the Commission and the CMT found different results," it said.
The CMT ended the problem when it reduced  Telefonica's  wholesale prices by between 22 percent and
61 percent.

HD         EU Fines Telefonica  S.A. EUR 151.9 Million  on ADSL-Reuters

Date Announced: 20070704

Reuters reported that  Telefonica  S.A. will have to pay EUR 151.9 million ($207 million) for squeezing out rivals from the ADSL Internet market, the EU's  antitrust authority stated on Wednesday. The European Commission stated, the Company's wholesale prices to other Internet providers were too high in comparison to its own retail prices -- a margin squeeze -- which meant rivals made losses when they resold the access to ordinary consumers. The Company has argued it was following market rules on pricing as established by Spain's telecoms market commission CMT, and that the EU was effectively overruling the national watchdog. But the Commission said CMT's pricing was based on the Company's forecasts.
