HD          Telecom supervisor chief fumes at Brussels' meddling
BY           A. S.
WC          298 words
PD           4 July 2007
SN           El Pais - English Edition
SC           PAISE
LA           English
CY           (c) 2007 EL PAIS, SL/IHT.

LP
Spain's telecoms regulator yesterday took umbrage at the European anti-trust authorities going over its head in a case involving Telefónica.

A brewing row over whether  Telefónica  was guilty of trying to stymie competition in the Spanish broadband market is expected to come to a head later this week with the European Commission slapping a huge fine on the former state monopoly for overcharging rivals for the use of its ADSL network in order to squeeze their margins.

TD
The Telecommunications Market Commission (CMT) had previously launched its own probe in 2003 following a complaint by a rival, and decided to shelve the case after finding that  Telefónica  had adhered to the wholesale ADSL pricing system the CMT itself had established.

"As head of the CMT, I don't take kindly to this," the chairman of the watchdog, Reinaldo Rodríguez said. "I believe we independent regulators know the sector better [than Brussels] and have better tools [to promote  competition],"he added.

If the Commission feels that there are grounds for taking legal action, it should do so against the CMT, not Telefónica,  Rodríguez said, and predicted that the dispute would have to be settled by the European Court of Justice. According to sources close to the EC,  Telefónica  could be fined over
€100 million.

Rodríquez defended the CMT's track record in freeing up the telecoms market. He pointed to the fact that Telefónica's  market share of the ADSL business in Spain had fallen to 55-56 percent due to the development of alternative networks from 95 percent when it decided to shelve the complaint filed by France Telecom's  Wanadoo.

However, he noted that broadband rates still remain above the European average, particularly for retail customers.

HD          Telefonica  SA - European Commission decision
WC        742 words
PD         4 July 2007
ET          02:26 PM
SN         Regulatory News Service
SC         RNS
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007

LP
RNS Number:6561Z  Telefonica  SA 04 July 2007
PRESS RELEASE

TD
The company has fully complied with the Spanish authority's regulation

TELEFONICA DISAGREES WITH  EUROPEAN COMMISSION DECISION AND WILL IMMEDIATELY REQUEST ITS ANNULMENT BEFORE THE EUROPEAN COURTS

The EU decision is wholly unjustified given the satisfactory evolution of the Spanish broadband market, publicly acknowledged by the national regulator.

Madrid, 4 July 2007.- Telefonica  announced today its firm intention to lodge an application for annulment of the European Commission ruling in connection with the proceedings initiated against Telefonica  de Espana for an alleged abuse of dominance in the Spanish market for broadband Internet access. It will file its request before the European Courts in the coming days.

Telefonica  considers the Commission decision unjustified and disproportionate.  Telefonica  contests the decision's legal, economical and market analysis, and underlines that it has fully complied with the existing regulation imposed by the Spanish authority, the Comision del Mercado de las Telecomunicaciones (CMT). The company is convinced it has rigorously met the CMT's strict parameters which, for the sake of  competition and on an ex ante basis, regulate every initiative taken by Telefonica  to offer consumers a range of innovative and varied products and services, both in terms of price and quality.

It is the first time that the Commission identifies an illegal margin squeeze for a non-essential service such as wholesale indirect access, which does not exist as a regulatory obligation in some of our neighbouring countries. Moreover, the Commission's line of argument is totally contradictory, since high retail prices by definition provide competitors with a margin.

Furthermore, as publicly acknowledged by the CMT, the Spanish broadband market has seen increased competition, which makes the Commission decision even more surprising. New operators have entered the market while  Telefonica's  share of the broadband market has decreased steadily (from 95% in 2001 to the current 55%). To  fine Telefonica  for a breach of the  competition rules is not reasonable.

Spain has experienced an exponential growth in broadband connections between 2001 and 2005, in line with similar EU Member States. The  European Commission itself recognised this in its latest implementation report, stating that broadband penetration in Spain is developing satisfactorily.

The Spanish broadband market is characterised by the presence of strong and well-capitalised competitors, including cable operators who do not depend on  Telefonica's  infrastructure to offer their services. This differentiates the Spanish market from many other EU countries, further calling into doubt the Commission's decision.
Legal uncertainty


Page 75 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators - the national regulator (CMT) and the European Commission - which are at odds with each other. As a result, the decision by the Commission creates enormous uncertainty about the role played by the regulatory bodies and the competition authorities in the telecommunications sector, throwing into question the supervisory functions of the Spanish authorities.

The legal uncertainty created by this decision will inevitably affect  Telefonica's  and other operators' ability to launch new products and services, with a direct impact on the entire European telecommunications sector to the detriment of European consumers.

Telefonica  will nevertheless continue to offer broadband services in the highly competitive Spanish market, while at the same time maintaining its commitment to guaranteeing fair  competition and generating consumer benefits as core values within  Telefonica's  corporate strategy.

The events date back to 2003, when Wanadoo (a company with public participation of the French State) lodged a complaint before the European Commission against Telefonica  alleging an abuse of dominant position in the Spanish broadband market through a so-called "margin squeeze." In February
2006, the  European Commission initiated formal proceedings against  Telefonica  de Espana on the grounds of anti-competitive behaviour, without having considered the possibility of imposing precautionary measures to remedy the situation. These proceedings were initiated despite recurring and intense supervision by the Spanish telecommunications regulator CMT, which has monitored and regulated the company's pricing policy for broadband services without ever detecting a margin squeeze. For this reason, Telefonica  has legitimate reasons to believe it has strictly adhered to the regulations governing fair competition and has acted in good faith.

HD        Company News Alert - Telefónica  Receives EUR151mn Fine For Broadband Dominance
WC        427 words
PD         5 July 2007
SN         Business Monitor International
SC         EMDN
LA         English
CY         © Copyright 2007 Business Monitor International.

LP
Telefónica  has been fined EUR151mn by the  European Commission for anti-competitive behaviour in the Spanish broadband market. As reported by BMI in late June, the penalty was confirmed in a draft decision, which was approved at a meeting of Commissioners on July 4. The sum is the second-highest fine ever handed out to a company for abusing its dominant market position, after the EUR497mn penalty handed out to Microsoft.

TD
The Spanish telecoms group was found guilty of using its leading position in the local broadband market to operate a margin squeeze on its competitors. In other words, the difference between the wholesale charges levied on alternative operators and the retail price of  Telefónica's  own services was so small that its rivals were unable to make their operations economically viable. Commenting on the  fine, Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said that 'when consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers. I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation.' Improving accessibility to broadband services is a key pillar of the EU's strategy to transform Europe into a high-tech knowledge economy. The Commission argues that Telefónica's  dominance is one of the main reasons why Spain has a lower broadband penetration rate than other Western European markets such as France or the UK.

However, there is some good news for Telefónica  in the mobile sector. According to news reports, the group's European wireless unit Telefónica  O2 is close to securing an exclusive deal to supply Apple 's iPhone in the UK market. The phone was recently launched in the US, amid much media hype, by AT&T. Apple is now preparing to launch the handset in three key European markets - the UK, France
and Germany - before expanding elsewhere in Europe and into Asia. O2 is the front runner to secure an iPhone deal in the UK, given that it is the market leader. At the end of 2006, the operator had 17.6mn subscribers, compared with 16.94mn at Vodafone and 16.9mn at T-Mobile. However, with all three operators so close in terms of subscribers and b>Orange not far behind on 15.3mn, Apple has a wide choice of partners, putting it in a strong bargaining position. Indeed, the computer and media group is already thought to have struck a hard bargain with AT&T, reportedly securing a share of the operator's customer revenues.

HD      COMPETITION : SPAIN'S TELEFONICA FINED RECORD 151 MILLION EURO WC    675 words
PD     5 July 2007
SN     Europolitics SC     EURREP LA     English
CY     Copyright 2007 Europe Information Service All Rights Reserved

LP
The European Commission's competition team marked 4 July by fining Spanish telecoms giant Telefonica  €151.875 million, the second-highest  fine ever imposed in an abuse of dominance case (after, of course, Microsoft).

"Telefonica  has committed a very serious  abuse of its dominant position," said  Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes. "I will not allow dominant companies to use their market power to close down markets that the European Union has opened."

TD
As the former telecoms incumbent prior to liberalisation,  Telefonica  is the largest internet provider in Spain. It is the only Spanish operator with a network covering the whole country and thus holds a dominant position in the market. The Commission's investigation found that, from 2001 to 2006, the company ran a pricing strategy that effectively stopped competitors advancing in the broadband internet
market. Telefonica  was charging competitors a fee for access to internet infrastructures which was almost the same as the price paid by consumers for using the service. This strategy effectively blocked rivals
from competing with  Telefonica  by preventing them from setting prices high enough to leave room for any profit. It also meant that no new entrants could hope to get even a foothold. If  Telefonica  themselves had been paying their own access fees, they would have clocked up considerable losses, the Commission found. This practice of so-called margin squeeze' constitutes an  abuse of the company's dominant
position contrary to Article 82 of the Treaty.

The overall effect, said Kroes, was to remove competitive incentives to lower prices, invest in infrastructure and introduce innovative services. This left Spanish consumers paying on average 20% more for broadband than their EU neighbours and has led to a penetration rate for such services in Spain trailing below the EU average.

And the commissioner had a wider message for other formerly state-run sectors - although she would not specify, energy is an obvious candidate. "I want to send a strong signal to dominant undertakings in all sectors that could be tempted to engage in similar practices that I will not tolerate such behaviour," she said.
THE SPANISH MARKET

Telecoms is, indeed, a regulated market and, said Kroes, it was action by the Spanish telecoms regulator CMT which put an end to the  abuse. Following a statement of objections sent by the Commission to Telefonica  in February 2006, CMT carried out their own analysis. This led to them setting new maximum wholesale prices in December 2006, thus opening the margin for possible profit. Just as well they did so, commented Kroes, as, had the  abuse continued,  Telefonica  would have found itself with an even higher fine.

The Commission was at pains to explain that there was no conflict with the CMT over the case, even though the results of the two investigations appear at variance. The regulators, explained a Commission memo, were judging the effectiveness of  competition ex ante using forecasts based on figures provided by Telefonica  itself. The Commission, however, was acting ex poste on the basis of actual cost figures and with the benefit of hindsight.
WARNINGS IGNORED


Page 80 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Telefonica  is by no means the first telecoms company to try to benefit from its former monopoly position. In May 2003, the Commission  finedDeutsche Telekom €12.6 million for similarly fixing prices below cost on internet markets. And two months later,  Wanadoo, a subsidiary of French incumbent France Télécom, was fined €10.35 million for the very same margin squeeze practices. In fact it was this  fine which prompted Wanadoo to make the complaint which led to the opening of the  Telefonica  investigation in
July 2003. And the Spanish company's blatant failure to take notice of the Commission's clear message from these cases, said Neelie Kroes, was a factor in the level of the  fines. The upshot has been a  fine for Telefonica  at over ten times the 2003 levels. The Commission is getting tougher and, as Kroes pointed out, the sums levied in 2003 were clearly "not a sufficient deterrent".

HD     Expansion: Brussels fines Telefonica  151m euros (Bruselas  castiga a Telefonica  con una macromulta tras irrumpir en Europa).

LP
Yesterday, the European Commission fined Telefonica,  the Spanish telecommunications group,
151.8m euros for 'abuse of dominant market position in the Spanish broadband internet market'. The  fine
was the second-largest ever imposed in the European Union, after the  fine imposed on software giant
Microsoft (497m euros), and the largest ever imposed on a telco.

According to the Commission,  Telefonica  was guilty of providing rivals with wholesale access to its broadband network at such high prices that they were not able to cover the cost of providing internet services to retail customers. Brussels was of the view that this meant  Telefonica's  rivals had to incur losses or leave the market.  Telefonica  for its part, will appeal what it has described as the 'inexplicable' fine before the Court of Justice in Luxembourg, on the grounds that it is 'unjustified and disproportionate'.

HD     Expansion: Clashing  regulations behind Brussels 152m-euro fine on Telefonica  (Alta politica y baja seguridad).
WC    341 words
PD     5 July 2007
SN     Expansión SC     EXPNAB LA     English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Expansion ©
2007 All rights reserved.

LP
The 152m-euro fine imposed by the European Commission on leading Spanish telecoms group Telefonica  for abuse of dominant position in the internet broadband market has been considered a matter of state in business circles. The  fine, a result of a complaint lodged by France Telecom, in which the French state holds a stake, is fifteen times higher than the fine imposed on the French operator for the same reasons. It is also some fifteen times higher than the fine imposed on Deutsche Telekom, in which the German state holds a stake. France and Germany have a strong presence in European bodies, while the Spanish economy has been increasing rapidly over the last few years, taking also into account the recent substantial acquisitions carried out by Spanish companies in other European countries. Warnings and sanctions from Brussels to Spanish companies have increased over the last few months, mainly because of the tax incentives given to Spanish companies investing abroad.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the European commissioner for  competition, has been extremely hard on  Telefonica.  The operator has nearly doubled its size in barely three years, having entered the UK and German markets through the acquisition of mobile operator O2, and also the Czech market. Ms Kroes yesterday sent to Cesar Alierta, the chairman of  Telefonica,  a brief six-paragraph fax with the reasons behind the  fine and the amount to be paid - 152m euros, i.e. 2.4 per cent of the group's net profit in 2006 -, adding that there will be a 3.5 per cent interest charge if the fine is not paid within three months. The CMT, the Spanish stock market regulator, is defending Telefonica's  position, as it considers that Brussels wants to weaken the power of the CMT and other domestic regulators. The clashing regulations between Brussels and the CMT result in legal insecurity for  Telefonica  when it comes to defending its business.

SE        COMPANIES EUROPE
HD        Best of enemies EUROPEAN VIEW - PAUL BETTS. BY        By PAUL BETTS
WC       346 words
PD        5 July 2007
SN        Financial Times
SC        FTFT
ED        London Ed1

LA         English

LP
The European Commission's decision yesterday to make an example of  Telefonica  for abuses of its dominant position in the broadband market is a fairly serious blow for the Spanish telecoms group.

Although the company is big enough to absorb the hefty Euros 152m (Dollars 207m)  fine - assuming its appeal fails - the sanction undermines a concerted campaign to shed its image in Spain as the big, bad market-abusing incumbent.

TD
Competitors and consumers readily agree that, in some cases, the company's marketing and pricing techniques are questionable at best and, yes, abusive at worst. Ironically, however, in the case of network access charges to broadband competitors,  Telefonica  appears to have been playing by the rules.

Executives have always maintained their innocence and those from some rival groups admit privately that Brussels may have come down a little hard on the incumbent.

Spain's Telecoms Market Commission (CMT), the market regulator, has been particularly critical of the sanction, arguing that, as it sets wholesale tariffs for fixed-line services, the scope for  abuse is minimal. When the tariff system was introduced in 2001, it argues,  Telefonica  enjoyed a whopping 95 per cent of the fixed-line market. This has since been pared to 55 per cent.

Although conceding that the country's broadband market has been slow to evolve, its system for calculating network access charges - based on subtracting from the final cost to consumers - is not the culprit, it argues.

The fact that the CMT has taken offence at the EC's charges against  Telefonica  is not surprising: the sanction is akin to a slap in the face for the regulator.

But it has given rise to a most surprising alliance. In normal circumstances, the CMT and  Telefonica  are at each other's throats, the former disallowing unfairly priced products and the latter complaining of overzealousness. Suddenly, they are comrades in arms. Whatever  Telefonica  and the CMT say about the EC, they may one day thank it for bringing them together.

CO        comtn : Telefonica SA

SE       COMPANIES EUROPE
HD        Telefonica  hit by Euros 152m antitrust fine. BY       By TOBIAS BUCK and MARK MULLIGAN
WC      487 words
PD       5 July 2007
SN       Financial Times
SC       FTFT
ED       London Ed1
PG       Page 24
LA       English
CY       (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Telefonica  was yesterday told to pay a Euros 152m (Dollars 207m)  antitrust fine, after the  European   Commission found Spain's dominant telecoms group guilty of illegally freezing out rivals from the lucrative broadband market.

It is the largest  fine Brussels has ever imposed on a telecoms company, outstripping earlier penalties imposed on Deutsche Telekom and France's  Wanadoo for similar abuses by a factor of 10. With the exception of Microsoft, no other company has been punished more severely for abusing a dominant position.

TD
The EU's top  antitrust regulator said the fine was intended as a warning to other groups, and reflected Brussels' determination to defend competition in newly liberalised markets such as energy and telecoms.  Telefonica  rejected the charges, and vowed to appeal against the ruling.

"Telefonica's  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy," said Neelie Kroes, the EU  competition commissioner. "I want to send a strong signal to dominant undertakings in all sectors that could be tempted to engage in similar practices that I will nottolerate such behaviour," she added.

The Commission found  Telefonica  operated a "margin squeeze" on its rivals for more than five years beginning in September 2001. The group weakened its competitors by setting the wholesale cost of accessing Telefonica's  broadband network so close to the retail price that rivals were forced to make losses to stay in the market,Brussels said.

Ms Kroes said the group's behaviour had undermined the growth of broadband in one of Europe's largest economies: "Spain has a broadband penetration rate well below the EU average. Consumers have been put off from exploring the new services that broadband internet allows."

Telefonica  yesterday railed against the size of the  fine, describing it as "unjustified and disproportionate".

The Commission's case against  Telefonica  has also raised questions about the regulation of Spain's broadband segment, where network access charges are fixed by the Telecoms Market Commission (CMT). The group has repeatedly pointed to the CMT's approval of its rates in its defence against Brussels, exposing an embarrassing rift between the Spanish regulator and the Commission.

Telefonica  said it had always "complied scrupulously with the rules imposed by the CMT". It added: "We believe that the  fine punishes us for being caught between two sets of contradictory rules - those of the CMT and those of the European regulator." Until late 2003, retail charges were set by the Spanish government, and the CMT used these to calculate wholesale charges which were supposed to allow space for competitors to make money in the broadband market.

When retail prices were liberalised, it began calculating the wholesale tariffs according to real market prices provided by Telefonica.
Page 86 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The company said it would present its appeal "within the next few days".

SE        BUSINESS
HD         Telefonica fined €152m over broadband prices
BY        By Nic Fildes
WC       538 words
PD        5 July 2007
SN        The Independent
SC        IND

LP
BUSINESS

The European Commission has fined Telefonica,  the Spanish incumbent telecoms company and owner of O2, a record €152m (£102m) over its wholesale broadband pricing, prompting a furious response from the company.

TD
The fine is a record for the telecoms sector and the second largest ever imposed by EU regulators, beaten only by the €497m penalty dished out to Microsoft for abusing its market position. It dwarfs previous penalties of tens of millions of euros imposed on Deutsche Telekom and France Telecom’s Wanadoo unit in 2003 over similar issues.

Telefonica  has been accused of abusing its market position in Spain by charging its competitors in the broadband sector excessive fees for its wholesale product compared to its own retail prices. EU regulators have said that this stifled  competition, as Telefonica’s  rivals in the broadband market were not able to make a sufficient margin when reselling high-speed internet services to consumers.

Neelie Kroes, the European Union  competition commissioner, said: “Telefonica’s  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe’s economy.”

Ms Kroes defended the size of the  fine, arguing that previous financial sanctions imposed over market abuse were not sufficient to act as a deterrent to other European companies. She said that small businesses and consumers in Spain pay around 20 per cent more than the EU average for high-speed internet and that Telefonica  could have “ended this  abuse” when the inquiry began in late 2003 after a complaint from Wanadoo.

Ms Kroes has taken a tough stance over anti-competitive issues since she took over as EU  competition commissioner in 2004 and said the  fine imposed on the Spanish telecoms provider should act as a warning to other incumbent or dominant players in industries such as energy and telecoms. “I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation.”

Telefonica  said it would immediately appeal the  European Commission’s ruling and will file an appeal with the European Court of Justice over the coming days. In a strongly worded statement,  Telefonica said the decision to  fine the company was “wholly unjustified and disproportionate”. The company argues that it has complied with the regulation imposed by CMT, the Spanish regulator, and that competition in the broadband market has been increasing. “Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators which are at odds with each other,” it said.

Telefonica  also said that its share of the broadband market has “decreased significantly and steadily in recent years”. It also said that the  European Commission itself has recognised the growth in competition in Spanish broadband in its latest implementation report, which stated that high-speed internet penetration in Spain is “developing satisfactorily”. The company argued that the market is characterised by the presence of strong competitors, including cable operators that do not depend on its wholesale product.


Page 88 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Mark James, an analyst with Collins Stewart, said that the fine was “anomalous” given that wholesale prices were set by the Spanish regulator, not by Telefonica.  Mr James said that while the  fine was unwelcome, it only represented 0.2 per cent of the compa-ny’s market value.

HD            Telefonica fined Euro 151m for abuse of market position;World Business

Telefonica,  the Spanish telecoms operator, was  fined Euro 151 million (Pounds 102 million) by the European Commission yesterday for implementing anti competitive practices against its domestic rivals between September 2001 and December 2006.

The fine is the highest -after a Euro 497 million penalty given to Microsoft to be imposed on a company for abusing its dominant position.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the European  Competition Commissioner, said that  Telefonica's  behaviour had raised costs for competitors. "When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers. I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation," she said.

Telefonica  was found guilty of operating a margin squeeze between the high wholesale rates that it charged rivals wishing to use its broadband access and the retail prices that it set for its own customers.

The telecoms group said that it would appeal against yesterday's decision, which it described as "unjustified and disproportionate". "Telefonica  has legitimate reasons to believe it has strictly adhered to the regulations governing fair competition and has acted in good faith," the company said.

HD       Telefónica  hit with record antitrust fine
BY      Matt Chapman
WC     249 words
PD      5 July 2007
SN      VNUNet United Kingdom

CY      Copyright 2007 VNU Business Publications. All Rights Reserved.

LP
Operator outraged by 'unjustified and disproportionate' €152m  fine
The European Commission has ordered Telefónica  to pay a record fine for antitrust activities in the
Spanish broadband market.

TD
The €152m (£103m) fine was imposed over the Spanish telco's wholesale broadband pricing, which the
Commission ruled is holding prices at 20 per cent higher than other European companies.

Telefónica  hit back at the  fine describing it as "wholly unjustified and disproportionate" as it had complied with competition guidelines set out by the Spanish Telecommunications Market Commission.

European Union  competition commissioner Neelie Kroes said that the  fine had been increased dramatically from those handed to Deutsche Telekom and France Telecom in 2003 because they had not acted as a deterrent to other companies.

"Telefónica's  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy," said Kroes.
However, Telefónica  said that the ruling would have wider effects for the broadband market throughout
Europe.

"This decision creates a legal insecurity that will alter the future performance of  Telefónica  and all the operators, with a direct hit in the development of the sector and damage to European end users," said the firm in a statement.

The €152m fine is the largest awarded against a telecoms company. Microsoft's  antitrust fine of €497m is the only higher amount imposed by the EU courts.

Telefónica,  which owns mobile phone company O2, said it would appeal the ruling.

HD     EU Fines Telefónica  US$207 mil. for Broadband Abuses
BY      Panos Loukos
WC     952 words
PD      5 July 2007
SN      Global Insight Daily Analysis
SC      WDAN
LA      English
CY      Copyright 2007, Global Insight Limited. All Rights Reserved.

LP
Global Insight Perspective
Significance               The
European  Commission hit Telefónica with the highest-ever penalty applied to a telecoms operator for abusing its dominant position in the local market. Implications               The local regulatory authority had approved the operator's charges and has thus come into direct confrontation with the European body.
Outlook                    Global Insight expects that this decision will
cause further problems for the European Union Information Society Commissioner
Viviane Reding, who favours the creation of a super-regulator on a European level.

TD
According to European Union (EU) officials in Brussels,  Telefónica—taking  advantage of its former incumbent operator status—fixed its broadband wholesale prices too high and did not leave enough margin for competing Internet Service Providers (ISPs) to make a profit, thus undermining  competition in the market. As a result, Spanish broadband access subscribers paid an estimated 20% more than the average monthly price in the 15 EU member countries before the penultimate enlargement in 2004. This reflects the broadband penetration of the country, which is 20 percentage points lower than the average EU figure due to 30% lesser growth rates. The European Union's  competition commissioner, Neelie Kroes, declared that, "I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation" and added that, "the margin squeeze that Telefónica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs, but also harmed customers significantly". Telefónica's  spokesman, Ángel Garzón, remarked that "we don't see any justification for this accusation, nor for any type of  fine". Garzón also observed that, "we've implemented all the requirements imposed by the Spanish regulator". The operator is expected to file an appeal within the next two months and 10 days, as outlined by the law.
Historical Background And The Regulator's Position

Yesterday's  fine resulted from anti-trust claims made by France Telecom, then operating under the Wanadoo brand, dating back to 2003, which accused  Telefónica  of fixing its resale broadband prices too high. The accusation involved the period between September 2001 and December 2006, despite the fact that all prices were approved by Spanish telecoms regulator Comisión del Mercado de las Telecomunicaciones (CMT). The  European Commission announced that it does recognise the fact that resale prices were regulated by to the law, although it claims that  Telefónica  could have lowered its rates of its own accord, knowing that the profit margin was limited. After changes in the process, CMT analysed the market status in February 2006 and decided to lower  Telefónica's  resale prices in December 2006, which brought an end to the claims. According to Brussels, CMT allowed the Spanish incumbent to apply the same pricing strategy for over five years and this resulted to this week's  fine. CMT's president, Reinaldo Rodríguez, announced that the Spanish regulator's position was commensurate neither with the methodology nor with the European Commission's applied criteria to the  fine. Rodriguez claimed that
the fine refers to a period during which Telefónica  claimed an approximate 95% of the broadband market, whereas now its share has fallen to 56%. He also added that the  European Commission conforms to the  Competition Law, which involves legislation other than that applied by the national regulatory authorities. This law, he indicated, has not been capable of promoting  competition, and he further argued that the EU shoud not rule on cases that have already been assessed by the national regulators.
Page 96 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Outlook and Implications
Landmark Fine

The fine is the highest ever imposed by the European body on a telecoms operator and is several times higher than similar penalties imposed in the past. For example, the Commission applied a 12.6 million euro (US$14.76 million)  fine against German incumbent Deutsche Telekom for unfair pricing practices when providing access to its local loops (see Germany: 21 May 2003: ). According to the European body, Deutsche Telekom charged companies higher fees for wholesale access to the local loop than the price subscribers paid for their fixed-line subscriptions, thus discouraging new players from entering the market and reducing price competition for consumers. It also  finedFrance Telecom 10.4 million euro after
finding that its broadband-access charges were below its costs. Under EU rules, the Commission can impose penalties that can amount to as much as 10% of global annual sales for abusing their market positions under the common  Competition Law.
Super-Regulator Versus.National Authorities
This latest development is in line with the EU's plans to proceed with the creation of a single
super-regulator, as outlined by Information Society Commissioner Viviane Reding (see Europe: 1 June
2007: ). In a statement released on 31 May, Reding said she will launch the proposals by end of October
2007, seeking further powers to strengthen telecoms regulations in the region. "Telecommunications is
clearly a field where we need more Europe, not less," she said. "In many EU countries, I see that national
telecoms regulators are prevented from doing the best possible job, either because they are not
sufficiently independent or lack the required resources and instruments—it is here where Europe can
help". Reding also plans to force incumbent telcos to spin off their local-loop infrastructure. In relation to
Telefónica's  fine, CMT's president said that this is not part of the Commission's role, because "to take
such a decision you have to play by the same rules of the game" and that "European independent
regulators are specialised on the telecommunications sector, we know it better and we apply better
practices. Deciding over the correctness or not of the resolution of a certain commission…can serve as a
reference to the European Court of Justice".

HD      EU Competition Authority Issues Landmark  Fine to Spanish Telecoms Giant
BY      Rachna Uppal
WC     305 words
PD      5 July 2007
SN      Global Insight Daily Analysis
SC      WDAN
LA       English
CY      Copyright 2007, Global Insight Limited. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Commission has issued a record fine to Spain's  Telefonica  for abuse of market position regarding the telecoms operator's broadband services. The Commissioner for Competition, Neelie
Kroes, approved the 151.9-million-euro  fine (US$206 million) for  Telefonica's  anti-competitive practices regarding Spain's broadband Internet market. Kroes accused the telecoms giant of "a very serious  abuse of its dominant position" by forcing rival broadband providers to buy wholesale broadband access from
the company, which, it is claimed, kept prices "artificially high", thereby squeezing the margins of its competitors.  Telefonica  is the only Spanish telecommunications operator that has a nation-wide fixed telephone network, which gave it a dominant market position.

TD
The Commission highlighted that as a result of such practices, "Spanish consumers are paying far more than the average for high-speed Internet access and many have chosen not to pay that price."Significance: The  European Commission is the European Union’s (EU)'s top  competition watchdog, and is fierce in safeguarding the liberalisation of markets within the bloc, particularly targeting the energy, telecommunications and transport sectors.  Fines are deemed to be an effective deterrent to anti-competitive practices, but in the past these have been more a symbolic gesture than anything else. The Commission has also often clashed with member-states over  competition, with the latter keen to defend "national champions" from exposure to full market liberalisation. One such example is the recent initiative from French President Nicolas Sarkozy to delete a crucial reference "fair and undistorted competition" from one of the basic principles of the EU charter. This has sent warning signals to the Commission, concerned at an erosion of its powers of regulation and supervision, which it has vowed to fight at all cost.

HD          Leading the News: Telefonica  is fined by EU over broadband pricing
BY           By Adam Cohen
WC          478 words
PD           5 July 2007
SN           The Wall Street Journal Europe
SC           WSJE
LA           English
CY           (Copyright (c) 2007, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

LP
BRUSSELS -- European Union regulators finedTelefonica SA 151.9 million euros ($206.9 million), saying the company used its grip Spain's telephone network to hamper rival providers of high-speed Internet services.

By raising fees for access to its telephone network and lowering its own retail broadband prices, Telefonica  squeezed other providers' margins, said the  European Commission, the EU's executive arm.

TD
The commission's penalty -- the highest against a telecommunications company -- is part of a broader effort to shake up the sector, still dominated by former state-owned monopolies. During the past year, the regulator has pressed for cuts in mobile-phone roaming charges and changes in several broadband markets. Last week it took Germany to court for giving Deutsche Telekom AG a legal monopoly over the country's broadband network.

Telefonica  was "making it virtually impossible for competitors to enter the market," the commission's top antitrust official, Neelie Kroes, told a news conference.

The company said it will appeal the  fine, calling the commission's penalty "unjustified and disproportionate." It added that it has complied with rules established by Spain's telecom regulator.

"Telefonica  finds itself squeezed between two regulators, the national regulator and the  European     Commission, which are at odds with each other," the company said in a statement.

Ms. Kroes said  Telefonica's  fine was justified because of the seriousness of the  abuse and the harm to consumers. Spanish broadband customers pay 20% more than the average price in the 15 oldest
EU member countries, and Spain's broadband penetration rate is 20% below average, the commission said.
The hefty penalty shows the EU regulator is escalating its crackdown on  antitrust abuses.

In similar cases in 2003,  Wanadoo, the Internet arm of France Telecom SA, was  fined 10.4 million euros and Deutsche Telekom 12.6 million euros. In a range of recent alleged collusion cases,  fines have been several times larger than in comparable cases just a few years ago.

The company said its share of Spain's broadband market has declined steadily since 2001, to 55% from 95%. Its main competitors for broadband services include Orange, a unit of France Telecom, and Jazztel PLC. Orange first complained to the commission about  Telefonica's  business practices in
2003.
The commission's  fine "confirms the validity" of Orange's complaint, Orange said in a statement. A
spokesman for Jazztel declined to comment.

Being a former state-owned monopoly helped  Telefonica  rig Spain's broadband market, the commission said. About 80% of high-speed Internet connections in Spain are made over fixed telephone lines.  Telefonica,  which was privatized in 1997, is the only Spanish telecommunications company with a nationwide fixed-line network.

HD     El Pais: Telefonica  to lodge appeal against Brussels fine (La compania  recurrira la sancion  ante
Luxemburgo por desproporcionada).

Following the decision by Brussels to impose a 151.875m-euro  fine on Telefonica  for abuse of dominant position in the internet broadband market, the leading Spanish telecoms group has announced that within the next few days it will be lodging an appeal against the decision with the Luxemburg Court of Justice, asking for the  fine to be cancelled.

TD
Telefonica  considers that the decision is both unjustified and disproportionate, as the operator as always followed the regulations set by the CMT, the Spanish telecoms market regulator. It also mentions that the fine has been imposed following a request from France Telecom, in which the French state holds a stake and which has already fined for the same reason. Telefonica  also argues that the  European                    Commission has mentioned the lack of competition in the Spanish market, when in fact the operator's share of this market has fallen from 95 per cent in 2001 to 55 per cent in 2006. The Spanish operator also rejects the accusation that it has hindered the development of broadband in the country, and it also adds that Telefonica's  prices stand around the European average. Finally,  Telefonica  wonders why, if the European authorities were so concerned about the rights of the consumers, it has taken them four years
to make a decision without adopting any cautionary measures.

SE       INTERNATIONAL INDEX - TOP STORY 2
HD       Telefonica  slapped with $205 mil EC fine
BY       By Leo Cendrowicz
CR       ONLINE WC      471 words PD       5 July 2007
SN       Hollywood Reporter
SC       HLYW
LA       English
CY       Copyright 2007, VNU eMedia Inc. All rights reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS -- Spanish telecoms giant  Telefonica  was fined €151 million ($205 million) Wednesday by the European Commission for stunting the Spanish broadband Internet market by stifling  competition.

The Commission -- the European Union's  antitrust authority -- said  Telefonica  charged wholesale rates that were almost as much as retail prices, preventing competing Internet providers from making a profit.

TD
EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said the high  fine reflected the fact that the Spanish firm was aware it was breaching EU rules. The  fine is the largest ever slapped on a telecoms operator in Europe and the second largest the EC has imposed for abusing market strength after a hitting Microsoft with a €497 million penalty in 2004.

Kroes said Telefonica,  Spain's biggest telephone company and former national monopoly, was guilty of "very serious abuse" of its dominance in broadband Internet access. Kroes said the penalty set an important precedent for companies in other industries such as energy, transport and telecom services
that have recently been liberalized. "Let me be perfectly clear: I will not allow dominant companies to use their market power to close down markets that the European Union has opened," she said.

Spanish consumers pay 20% more than the average in the 15 countries that were EU members before enlargement in 2004, the EC said. The number of broadband subscribers in Spain is 20% below the average of the 15 nations, it added.

Telefonica  controls the fixed line telephone network used for 80% of all ADSL broadband Internet connections. Rivals need to buy wholesale access from  Telefonica  to offer competing retail services. But Telefonica  structured its wholesale and retail prices in such a way that the margin between them did not allow rivals to compete in the market without making losses. "Through this so-called 'margin squeeze,' Telefonica  insulated itself from the rigors of  competition by making it impossible for alternative broadband suppliers to enter the market on a commercially viable basis," Kroes said.

Telefonica  immediately announced it would appeal the decision at the European Court of First Instance, the EU's second-highest court. It described the ruling as "inexplicable," and insisted it had abided with
the Spanish regulatory authority, CMT. "Telefonica  finds the decision by Brussels to be unjustified and disproportionate, both legally and economically," the company said in a statement.

Telefonica  said that the EC's demands contradicted ongoing supervision from the CMT, and this would eventually create wider problems for the telecoms sector. "The legal uncertainty created by this decision will inevitably affect  Telefonica's  and other operators' ability to launch new products and services," it said.

At the same time, France Telecom, whose unit Orange competes with  Telefonica,  said it may file a damages claim against the Spanish operator.

HD     (EU) EU/COMPETITION: Commission fines Telefónica  €151 million for abusing  its dominant position on broadband Internet access
WC    542 words
PD     5 July 2007
SN     Agence Europe
SC     AGEU
LA     English
CY     Not Available for Re-Dissemination, except as permitted by your subscriber agreement. (c) Agence
Europe, Brussels 2007. All rights reserved.

LP
Brussels, 04/07/2007 (Agence Europe) - On Wednesday 4 July, the European Commission fined the incumbent Spanish telecommunications operator,  Telefónica,  €151,875,000 for a very serious abuse of its dominant position on the Spanish broadband internet access market. By squeezing the margins between the wholesale prices charged to competitors and the retail prices charged to its own customers, the company weakened its competitors, making their continued presence and growth difficult.
According to  Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, this practice is alleged to have brought about a
20% increase in retail prices compared to the EU average, with the effect of dissuading Spanish
consumers from subscribing to broadband internet.  Telefónica,  for its part, has already challenged the
“legal, economic and commercial analysis of the decision” and has declared its intention of bringing an
appeal to the European Court of Justice.

TD
This is an example of a phenomenon known as a “tariff scissor”, in which a company holds a dominant position on the wholesale market for a product or service, whilst at the same time being involved in the retail market. This configuration allows it artificially to inflate wholesale prices, keeping retail prices at a low level. The margin losses on the “retail” side are made up for by profit on the “wholesale” side. But retail competitors, which are obliged to purchase from the same wholesaler, do not have this option, and are therefore priced out of the market. “This behaviour by  Telefónica  [between 2001 and 2006] has harmed Spanish consumers, businesses and the economy as a whole and, by extension, the economy of Europe”, Ms Kroes told the press. The  fine, which is 15 times higher than the one handed down to Wanadoo for a similar scenario in France in 2003 (EUROPE 8506), is justified by the serious nature of the abuse, and
also by the fact that lower  fines did not appear to have any deterrent effect.

The actions of the Commission, however, are not completely in line with those of the Spanish regulator, the Comisión del Mercado de las Telecomunicaciones (CMT), which had closed its investigation of 2003 (triggered by a complaint by  Wanadoo in Spain), having noted that  Telefónica  had stuck to the tariffs recommended by the CMT itself in 2001. “As president of the CMT, I do not appreciate [the Commission's initiative]”, explained Reinaldo Rodríguez, speaking to the Spanish daily newspaper El País. “We know the sector better [than Brussels]”. If the Commission takes the view that action must be taken, he said, then
this action should be taken against the CMT. The commissioner replied that she has no problem in principle with the actions taken by the CMT, but that she is obliged to note that this is a situation which is likely to cause confusion. “People have to know who the regulator is, and be able to trust that regulator”, put in Miguel Garzon of  Telefónica,  “otherwise it's a shambles, for investors and for everybody”. Telefónica  plans to table its appeal with the Court in the next few days. If applicable, the proceedings will then be opened at the Court of First Instance within a few months. (cd)

HD            Regulator  fines Spanish telco $240m
CR            AP
WC            349 words

SN             The Australian
SC             AUSTLN

LP
Regulation

EU regulators  fined Spain's  Telefonica  E151 million ($240 million), claiming the company unfairly squeezed rivals by setting wholesale internet prices too high to allow them to turn a profit.

TD
"Telefonica  weakened its competitors, making their continued presence and growth difficult," the European Commission said. "Competitors were forced to make losses if they wanted to match Telefonica's  retail prices."

It said Telefonica's  national and regional wholesale prices from 2001 to 2006 were set at a level that held back the rollout of broadband internet in Spain. As a result, the EU said, consumers there used the internet less and paid a fifth more than other western Europeans.

"When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," said EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes. "I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalisation."

Telefonica  -- Spain's former national monopoly -- argues that the Spanish market is healthy because new companies entered and its own market share dropped 10 percentage points to just over half today.

The fine is far higher than other anti-trust  fines on telecoms companies in similar cases, such as a E12.6 million penalty on Deutsche Telekom or a E10 million  fine against France Telecom's internet arm Wanadoo.

The EU defended the high level of the  fine, saying Telefonica's  actions warranted a severe sanction due to their "gravity and duration" and because it had prevented new companies offering broadband in Spain.

Most broadband connections in Spain are offered over fixed telephone lines on a network controlled by Telefonica,  so rivals have to buy wholesale access either at the local, regional or national level.

It said the difference between  Telefonica's  retail and wholesale prices from September 2001 to December 2006 did not allow competitors enough margin to cover costs. "This means that a competing provider of broadband that was just as efficient as  Telefonica  was faced with the choice of either exiting the market or incurring losses," the commission said.

HOLIDAY ON ICE Scots bosses too busy to take a break

A SURVEY from the Chartered Management Institute claims that most mid-level executives in Scotland are not taking their full holiday entitlements, adding to a total of 21 million days unused each year across the UK. Some 63 per cent of the Scots asked admitted they will not use their full entitlement this year, a figure that has increased by half since 2003. One in three blames their workload and 39 per cent work
on holiday to reduce pressure when they return.

TD

The majority, it claims, ignore calls to reduce their "carbon footprint", with only 8 per cent planning to offset the gas created heading for favourite spots like the Greek Islands and Canada.

HD      EUROPEAN COMMISSIONFINESTELEFONICA FOR DSL PRICE SQUEEZE WC     231 words
PD      5 July 2007
SN      TR Daily SC      TDAILY LA       English
CY      © Copyright 2007. Aspen Publishers. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Commission has fined Spanish incumbent  Telefonica  151 million Euro (U.S. $205 million) for imposing a “margin squeeze” on wholesale purchasers of its DSL (digital subscriber line) service.

Telefonica,  the commission found, charged wholesale prices from September 2001 to December 2006 for DSL service such that the margin between its retail prices and the wholesale prices were “insufficient to cover the costs that an operator as efficient as  Telefonica  would have to incur to provide retail broadband access.” Spanish regulators imposed new rates on the carrier in December 2006 after the commission’s opening of formal proceedings in February of that year.

TD
The commission said that the high wholesale costs weakened retail  competition in the broadband market, noting that Spanish consumers pay 20% more than consumers in the other largest European countries for broadband access. Spain’s broadband penetration rate is 20% below the “EU-15" average, and growth in broadband service is nearly 30% below that of the EU-15.

“The margin squeeze that  Telefonica  imposed on its competitors not only raised their costs but also harmed consumers significantly,” said Neelie Kroes, European Commissioner-competition. “When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers. I will not allow dominant companies to set prices that undermine telecoms liberalization.” - Brian Hammond, brian.hammond@wolterskluwer.com

BRUSSELS - The Associated Press

EU regulators  fined Spain's  Telefonica  SA 151.9 million euros ($206.5 million) on Wednesday, claiming the company unfairly squeezed rivals by setting wholesale Internet prices too high to allow them turn a profit.

TD
EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said the high fine - second only in EU monopoly  abuse penalties to the landmark 497 million euros ($613 million) fine on Microsoft Corp. - reflected the fact that Telefonica  knew it was breaking EU  antitrust rules.

"Telefonica's  price structures raised its competitors' costs, restricted  competition on the retail market, and made consumers pay the price," she told reporters, saying the decision was a warning to others in newly opened sectors - such as energy.

The company said it would appeal the EU decision it called "unjustified and disproportionate," and said it contradicted ongoing supervision from the Spanish telecom regulator. It said this created legal uncertainty about which regulator was in charge, which would inevitably affect its ability to launch new products and services.

"Telefonica  has legitimate reasons to believe it has strictly adhered to the regulations governing fair competition and has acted in good faith," it said. Controlling the network: Spain's former national monopoly controls the fixed line telephone network used for 80 percent of all ADSL broadband Internet connections. Rivals need to buy wholesale access from  Telefonica  to offer competing retail services.

The European Commission said these wholesale prices on regional and national networks were set so high from 2001 to 2006 that they held back the rollout of broadband Internet in Spain. As a result, the EU said consumers there use the Internet less and pay a fifth more than other western Europeans.

"When consumers and businesses are harmed in such a major market, the entire economy suffers," Kroes said. "Consumers have been put off from exploring the new services that broadband Internet allows."

The fine is far higher than other  antitrust fines on telecom companies in similar cases, such as a 12.6 million euros ($17 million) penalty on Deutsche Telekom AG or a 10 million euros ($13 million)  fine against France Telecom AG's internet arm  Wanadoo.

SE       Única
HD       Contradictory fine
WC      531 words
PD       7 July 2007
SN       El Pais - English Edition
SC       PAISE
LA       English
CY       (c) 2007 EL PAIS, SL/IHT.

LP
THE EUROPEAN Commission (EC) has imposed on Telefónica  (the leading Spanish telecommunications company, formerly a state monopoly, now privatized, but still in a dominant market position) a fine of - 152 million - the second largest in the history of European Union sanctions after that of almost - 500 million imposed on Microsoft - for serious  abuse of its dominant position in the Spanish broadband market. According to the European commissioner for  competition, Neelie Kroes, between
2002 and 2004 the Spanish operator (which has the only countrywide network for broadband ADSL Internet access) maintained a policy of excessively high wholesale prices, creating a margin squeeze effect that impeded the entry of competitors into the Spanish ADSL market. This  abuse "harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy."

TD
If the conflict is a serious one, and goes beyond the limits of an ordinary sanction to a company, it is because the abusive wholesale prices now being punished by the EC were set by Spain's national Telecommunications Market Commission (CMT) - so that the correct procedure would have been to sanction the CMT. This fact has been recognized by the Spanish regulatory body, which has come to the defense of  Telefónica.

The trickiest aspect of this fine is the legal insecurity it introduces into the communications market. In juridical and regulatory terms, Telefónica  has been applying prices approved by the CMT, which then bring a disproportionate  fine, so heavy it is hard to explain even in terms of the "exemplary" character claimed by Brussels. A company that operates in a regulated market cannot be subject to the contradictory directives of two regulatory bodies, which, it seems, do not even communicate with one another.

The EC has thus created a politically impossible situation, in which a national company will not be sure of complying with European law when it applies the prices imposed by its national regulator. Kroes must understand that, though in the back rooms of the EC many are calling, reasonably enough, for a single European telecommunications regulator, it is a mistake to seek confrontation with national regulators.
This course will lead the European market to chaos, and quickly, too.

At first sight it would seem that this impasse can only be resolved by political negotiation, and indeed the Ministry of Industry already ought to be working in this direction. But the disorder generated by Brussels - which may get a little worse yet, if the Luxembourg Court rejects the  fine imposed by the EC on
Deutsche Telekom for misconduct even more serious than that imputed to  Telefónica  - ought to be kept separate from the all-too-true imputations often made about the high cost and low quality of ADSL service in Spain. Indeed this service is expensive; it has a very low index of penetration, probably due to lack of competition, and it is not as fast as the service given in the rest of Europe. Perhaps  Telefónica does not deserve a fine of - 152 million for applying the norms of the national regulatory body; but it should, at least, be required to make more investments to improve the quality of its service.

SE          Única
HD          Spain may cross swords  with Brussels over Telefónica fine
BY          A. S.
WC         368 words
PD          7 July 2007
SN          El Pais - English Edition
SC          PAISE
LA           English
CY          (c) 2007 EL PAIS, SL/IHT.

LP
Government to take up issue with Commission if penalty unfair

The government said Friday it would consider taking up the huge  fine imposed by the European         Commission earlier this week on  Telefónica  with Brussels if it believed the Spanish telecoms giant was unfairly treated.

TD
"It's too early too make judgments because we haven't received the official notice yet from Brussels," Deputy Prime Minister María Teresa Fernández de la Vega said. "When we receive it we will study it of course in detail in order to gauge, above all, whether there may have been discrimination with respect to cases in other countries and act accordingly."

Brussels on Wednesday  fined Telefónica  €151.9 million for  abuse of its dominant position in the Spanish broadband high-speed internet access market. That was the largest ever  fine imposed on a telecoms company, and the second biggest after a penalty of just half a billion euros the Commission slapped on Microsoft.
For similar earlier offences, Brussels had  finedDeutsche Telekom €
12.6 million and France Telecom €10.4 million.

Announcing the punishment, the European commissioner for  competition, Neelie Kroes, said the fines imposed on France Telecom and Deutsche Telekom had been insufficient to dissuade other operators from anti-trust practices. "I hope that today's [Wednesday]  fine has a bigger impact," she said.

The Commission accused  Telefónica  of overcharging rivals for the use of its broadband network thereby making it difficult for them to compete whilst still making a profit. The practice is known as a margin squeeze. The case was prompted by a complaint from French Telecom unit  Wanadoo.
The Spanish Telecommunications Market (CMT) Commission launched its own probe into the
complaint but absolved  Telefónica  of wrongdoing.  Telefónica  said it had fully complied with the pricing
rules established by the CMT and would be appealing the Commission's ruling with the European
Court of First Instance.

The former state monopoly described the  fine as "disproportionate" and complained it had been caught in the crossfire between two regulators, a situation which created legal insecurity (see EDITORIAL page 2).
The CMT also complained about the Commission treading on its patch.

Yesterday, EU sources expressed surprise at the lack of response from the Spanish government to a
multi-million-euro fine imposed on Spanish telecommunications group Telefonica by the European        
Commission earlier in the week.  Telefonica  was fined 152m euros for abusing its dominant position in
the Spanish broadband internet market.

Rather than come out in defence of the Spanish operator, Maria Teresa Fernandez de la Vega, Spain's first deputy prime minister, merely indicated yesterday that her government will study the  fine imposed, in particular 'to assess whether  Telefonica  has been the target of discriminatory treatment in respect of
other countries and take the appropriate action'. Previously, the Commission had  fined French ISP Wanadoo and Deutsche Telekom, the German telecommunications group, 10m and 12m euros respectively for breaches of EU  competition law.

HD         Spain could contest  Telefonica fine - minister
WC        232 words
PD         8 July 2007
ET          07:09 PM
SN         Reuters News
SC         LBA
LA          English
CY         (c) 2007 Reuters Limited

LP
MADRID, July 8 (Reuters) - Spain may contest the size of a  fine imposed on telecoms firm  Telefonica by the European Union if it was shown to have received harsher treatment than companies elsewhere for similar practices, Spanish Industry Minister Joan Clos said on Sunday.

Telefonica  was fined 152 million euros ($206.7 million) by Brussels for squeezing out high-speed
Internet rivals.

TD
French company Wanadoo and Germany's Deutsche Telekom were  fined 10.35 million and 12.6 million euros, respectively, for similar practises, Clos told radio station Catalunya Informacio in an interview.

"We are analysing what is the justification of such a strong measure and we would take action in the event that we find different treatment between countries," Clos said, calling the  Telefonica fine a "spectacular amount".

The European Commission fined Telefonica  on grounds its wholesale prices to other Internet providers were too high in comparison to its own retail prices.

That created a margin squeeze that meant rivals made losses when they resold access to ordinary consumers, Brussels said.

The fine is the second-highest the European Union's top  antitrust regulator has levied in an "abuse of dominance" case, beaten by almost half a billion euros Microsoft had to pay in 2004. It is the highest fine for a telecoms company.

HD        FT.com site : Blunt optimism of Spanish telecoms  operator. WC       920 words
PD        9 July 2007
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA        English
CY        (c) 2007 The Financial Times Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Seated beneath a painting by Magritte, the Belgian surrealist artist, Cesar Alierta's conversation is anything but surreal.

The executive chairman of  Telefonica,  the world's fifth-largest telecoms company by market capitalisation, is absorbed in the gritty reality of his sprawling empire during an interview in his Madrid office with the Financial Times.

TD
Telefonica  has become an industry favourite for some investors, after delivering sector-leading growth trends.

In 2006, it reported revenue of 53bn ($72bn), which represents a 42 per cent increase, and net income of 6.2bn, up 40 per cent.
Such performance was fuelled by ambitious deal-making, but there was organic growth as well.

The question is whether this momentum can be maintained. The larger a company becomes, the harder it can be to outperform its peers.

Mr Alierta insists the outperform story will continue.  Telefonica  is promising to double its earnings per share by 2009, based on the 2005 level of 0.9.

But the executive chairman says the focus is on organic growth in  Telefonica's  core businesses in Europe and Latin America. The deal-making of the past five years may not be over, but it is expected to slow.

Reflecting on his acquisitions, Mr Alierta says: "We were looking for the right scale. Now we have the right scale in Latin America. We have the right scale in Europe?.?.?.?So we are concentrating on what we have."
That message is designed to reassure investors because industry analysts say the main risk for
Telefonica  is over-reach through more deal-making.

The potential scale of  Telefonica's  ambitions was underlined in April when it joined a consortium led by Italian financial institutions that is proposing to buy a controlling stake in Telecom Italia from Pirelli, the tyre-maker.

Telefonica  is planning an "industrial partnership" with Telecom Italia if the consortium's proposal is approved by regulators, which will enable both companies to realise cost savings by flexing their joint muscle when buying telecoms equipment such as phones.

But Mr Alierta admits  Telefonica's  decision to pay 2.3bn for a 10 per cent economic interest in Telecom Italia is also a defensive move. America Movil, the Latin American mobile phone group owned by Mexican billionaire Carlos Slim, had also been trying to buy the same stake from Pirelli.

That threatened a nightmare scenario for Mr Alierta in  Telefonica's  most important Latin American market. Mr Slim could combine Claro, America Movil's Brazilian mobile operator, with TIM Brazil, Telecom Italia's wireless subsidiary, to create the country's dominant player. Vivo,  Telefonica's  Brazilian mobile joint venture with Portugal Telecom, would lose its status as the leading wireless operator.

Page 121 of 186 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Mr Alierta plays down the prospect of  Telefonica  quickly seeking to buy out its partners in the consortium, highlighting how Italian politicians were keen for Telecom Italia to remain in domestic hands. But he does not rule out an eventual takeover.

Nevertheless, he has more immediate issues to deal with. The European Commission last week imposed a record fine on a telecoms company, telling Telefonica  to pay 152m for allegedly taking steps to freeze competitors out of the broadband market.

Speaking before the  fine was announced, Mr Alierta rejects the Commission's charges and says "we will win" an appeal.

Some of Telefonica's  European mobile businesses are feeling the pressure: notably those that are part of O2, the UK group bought for 26bn in 2005. O2 Germany and O2 UK saw operating income before depreciation and amortisation fall by 6 and 5 per cent respectively year on year in the first quarter of
2007 because of fierce  competition.

Mr Alierta expresses confidence in O2 UK, which is the largest operator in the country. It is set to be the exclusive UK network for Apple's iPhone, which he describes as a "good device" but not a "category killer".

Some analysts doubt the performance of  Telefonica's  fixed-line operations in Latin America, partly because of increasing  competition. Mr Alierta brushes aside their concerns, claiming  Telefonica  has an advantage over many rivals through its ability to offer mobile alongside fixed-line services.

He says he is "happy" with  Telefonica's  geographic reach. The company will increase its minority stake in China Netcom, the country's second-largest fixed-line phone company, from 5 to 10 per cent over the next 12 months, but Mr Alierta says he is not looking for acquisitions in Asia.

Telefonica's  net debt level is due to fall from 2.7 times operating income before depreciation and amortisation, to 2.5 times next year. Some analysts say it should come down further because of the foreign exchange and interest rate risk stemming from  Telefonica's  Latin American businesses.

Mr Alierta is only coy when asked about the secret of  Telefonica's  success and highlights early efforts to reduce labour costs and to innovate with internet television. But he acknowledges the company has benefited in Spain from more than a decade of strong economic growth second only to Ireland in the European Union.

Mr Alierta's predecessor bought many of the Latin American businesses that have become  Telefonica's engine, although the current executive chairman has also made significant acquisitions there.

He reveals he listens carefully to some competitors, including arch-rival Mr Slim, with whom he shares a passion for cigars.
"He is brilliant and that is the reason he is the wealthiest man in the world, yeah?"

SE          Única
HD          Government to back Telefónica  should  telecoms  giant appeal against EC fine
BY          A. E.
WC         267 words
PD          10 July 2007
SN          El Pais - English Edition
SC          PAISE
LA          English
CY          (c) 2007 EL PAIS, SL/IHT.

LP
The Spanish government is considering launching a legal appeal against the  European Commission's (EC) decision to  fine Telefónica  €151.9 million for abusing its dominant position in the broadband internet market, Industry Minister Joan Clos announced Monday.

Clos said that the government would not hesitate to join  Telefónica  in taking the case before the European Court of First Instance if there is evidence to suggest the EC had discriminated against Spain's former telecoms monopoly.

TD
"A fine like this has never been imposed before," Clos said in an interview with Catalunya Radio yesterday. "We are studying the ruling to try to find the justification for such drastic action."

Last Thursday, the  European Commission said it was imposing the record  fine on Telefónica  after finding the telecommunications group guilty of trying to stifle  competition in the Spanish broadband internet-access market. It said the company had charged excessively high wholesale rates for use of its broadband network, making it impossible for rivals to compete without incurring losses.

"Telefónica's  conduct harmed Spanish consumers, Spanish businesses and the Spanish economy as a whole, and by extension Europe's economy," the European Commissioner for  Competition Neelie Kroes said.

However, other companies have faced smaller  fines in the past. France Telecom's internet service provider Wanadoo, for example, was ordered to pay €10.4 million and Deutsche Telekom €12.6 million for similar cases in 2003.

Kroes argued that the larger sum demanded was because smaller  fines had failed to force companies to change their behavior.

HD     Expansion: Telefonica  to challenge  data used by Commission in imposing fine (Telefonica  rebate los datos que apoyan la multa de Bruselas  por abuso de dominio).
WC    160 words
PD     11 July 2007
SN     Expansión SC     EXPNAB LA      English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Expansion ©

LP
Telefonica,  the Spanish telecommunications group, is preparing its response to a report by the European Commission in which it justifies the scale of the multi-million euro  fine imposed on the group. Telefonica was fined 152m euros for abuse of a dominant position in the broadband market, the largest fine
imposed on a telecoms group to date.
Of the total figure, the Commission stated that only 90m euros was linked directly to the offense itself;
22m euros reflected  Telefonica's  economic capacity, while the remainder of the  fine, 40m euros, was
designed to act as a deterrent to the group and the sector.  Telefonica  intends to challenge the data
relating to its market share and pricing system, which formed the basis of the Commission's calculations.

HD       International Telecom
WC      505 words
PD       12 July 2007
SN       Communications Daily
SC       COMD
LA       English
CY       (c) 2007 Warren Publishing, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Commission (EC) launched an antitrust investigation into a proposed merger of two providers of GSM roaming data services, it said Wednesday. U.S. technology company Syniverse serves wireless telecommunications companies. The BSG Group's services include payment processing, data clearing, financial settlement and risk management for wired and wireless communications service providers. The businesses overlap only in the market for GSM roaming data clearing services, but the companies are among that market's leaders; in Europe, they compete directly, the EC said. A merger would reduce the number of players in Europe from three to two, it said. The Commission has 90 days to rule on whether the acquisition would harm the market.
------

TD
"Competition by litigation" does not work, leaders from alternative telecommunications providers told European Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes on Wednesday. Abusive behavior that recently led to Spanish incumbent Telefonica  being fined 152 million euros could recur across Europe if the
Commission does not ensure that high-speed and fiber networks are open to  competition from the start, European Competitive Telecommunications Association members said in a meeting with Kroes. The association wants an end to the "cycle of repeated  abuse and foreclosure by dominant incumbents," with regulators setting clear rules for how incumbents upgrade networks. In the 1990s, Internet market growth was spurred largely by alternative providers, as incumbents lagged behind, the group said. Now, as the historical providers exploit their power over the local loop to deny rivals access, they are regaining a strong advantage in the broadband market, the association said. Kroes should guard against a reversal
of Europe's trend toward open telecommunications markets, it said.
------

The U.K. privacy czar Wednesday slammed corporate chiefs for failing to guard workers' and customers' personal data. Launching his annual report in London, Information Commissioner Richard Thomas branded as "frankly horrifying" breaches the past year involving mobile operator Orange, banks, government agencies, public bodies, retailers and others. How, he asked, can online recruitment agencies let applicants see one another's forms, or let workers take unencrypted laptops holding customer account details from offices? He warned business and public officials to take their data protection duties more seriously or face enforcement actions and lose customer confidence.
------

Spending on fixed business data services in Europe will plateau in 2008 then slow drastically, consultant Analysys said Wednesday. Competitive pressures on suppliers and Ethernet's debut as an access technology for metropolitan networks and wide area networks mean companies will keep getting more bandwidth for the same money when they upgrade, said report author Margaret Hopkins. To survive, service providers will need new ways to generate revenue or they will have to consolidate, she said. Small to mid-sized businesses remain a largely untapped market because existing services and distribution networks do not meet their needs, but this is changing, the study said. And for them to make the shift assurances are needed for data customers not yet convinced that next-generation networks are as secure and reliable as legacy data networks, Analysys said.

HD          Spain CMT Asks Govt To Challenge EU's Telefonica Fine
WC         214 words
PD          12 July 2007
ET           06:41 PM
SN          Dow Jones International News
SC          DJI
LA           English
CY          (c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
MADRID (Dow Jones)--Spain's telecommunication regulator has requested the government bring an annulment action before the European Court of Justice against the EUR151.9 million  fine imposed by the European Commission on dominant telecommunications company  Telefonica  SA (TEF).

Earlier this month, the  European Commission fined Telefonica  on the grounds that the telecommunications giant used its grip on the country's telephone network to hamper rival broadband Internet providers.

TD
The CMT, as the regulator is also known, said in a press release that the commission never formally asked for the cooperation of the Spanish regulator, contravening European provisions.
The CMT said the commission decision "seriously and deeply affects" its regulatory competence. "This type of intervention jeopardizes the fulfillment of its objectives of regulation in the benefit of
Spanish consumers and promotion of effective  competition in the sector through the development of
telecommunications infrastructures alternative to those of the incumbent," the CMT said.
The European Commission opened a formal investigation into  Telefonica's  business practices in
February 2006. Telefonica  has said it will appeal the  fine.
Company Web sites:  www.cmt.es and www.telefonica.es
